Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis deals with topography optimisation for thin fluid film problems. The aim is to apply
topography optimisation to steady­state pressure driven flow problems where the pressure distri­
bution is determined by solving the Reynolds Equation under the assumptions of isothermal and
isoviscous fluid. The introduction of a orifice in the thin film yields a simple viscous pump subject
to further optimisation.
The thin film is modeled using a finite element formulation which is derived from the strong form of
Reynolds equation. The derived formulation is implemented in the multi­paradigm programming
language MATLAB.
The presented work is seen as providing new research to the field of topography optimisation
in tribology. The extension to topography optimisation of a orifice acting as a pump outlet in
thin fluid films appears poorly documented in literature thus this works aims to expand on this
topic. Various extensions of the finite element formulation of Reynolds equation with respect to
introducing a orifice are examined prior to optimisation. The presented results are academic in
nature but it is envisioned that further development of the methods could be employed to optimise
realistic problems such as a constant flow viscous pump.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
iii
==PAGE:6==
Resumé
Denne rapport beskriver topografi optimering af glidelejer og viskøse pumper.
Oliefilmen i glidelejerne er beskrevet som tidsuafhængig trykdreven fluid beskrevet med Reynolds
ligning under antagelse af isotermisk og isoviskøs fluid. En fluid pumpe opnås ved at placere et
hul i glidelejet.
Trykket i fluidfilmen løses numerisk via en finite element model, der er udledt af Reynolds ligning.
Den numeriske model er implementeret i programmeringssproget MATLAB.
Resultaterne i rapporten betragtes som værende ny forskning indenfor topografi optimering af
glidelejer. Resultaterne er af akademisk karakter, men forventes at kunne forbedres.

Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings

Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
The aim of this thesis is to apply topography optimisation to viscous pump geometries inspired by
thin film hydrodynamic thrust bearings.
Thin film is described as a steady­state pressure driven flow problem where the pressure distribu­
tion is determined by solving the Reynolds equation. The Reynolds equation describes the pressure
generated in a fluid between two surfaces undergoing constant shearing, which is a simple method
for generating vast pressure with minimal moving parts. To reduce complexity it is assumed that
steady­state refers to a time invariant film thickness and load carrying capacity.
Prior to optimising viscous pump geometries the Reynolds equation is derived and a numerical
method for obtaining the pressure distribution is formulated and implemented. A routine is imple­
mented to allow optimisation of surface topography of the plates containing the thin film. To ensure
the numerical method and optimisation routine are implemented correctly a series of infinitely wide
and finite width bearings are optimised for various objectives prior to the introduction of a pump
orifice.
The viscous pump optimisation covers both topography optimisation for fixed orifice shapes, sizes
and locations as well as topology optimisation of the plates containing the thin film.
1.1
Motivation
In hydraulic pumps such as gear and centrifugal pumps the gear teeth or impeller blades passes the
flow outlet which leads to pressure oscillations with the frequency and harmonics of the rotating
assemblies angular velocity. Figure 1.1a shows a simplified gear pump geometry from which it is
seen that the fluid is delivered to the outlet region as pockets of fluid between gear teeth leading
to the oscillations of outlet pressure p as shown in figure 1.1b. According to Brennen in [7] the
p
Qi
Qo
(a) Simplified gear pump geometry.
p
t
(b) Oscillating outlet pressure p with re­
spect to time t.
Figure 1.1: Simplified gear pump geometry with inlet flow Qi, outlet flow Qo and outlet pressure
p. Geometry and pressure is not physically correct in this figure.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
1
==PAGE:12==
oscillations caused by blade passing, which is the effect caused by an impeller blade passing the
outlet, can lead to resonances in the system often causing substantial damage.
To reduce the risk of substantial damage caused by blade passing a pump geometry which relies on
an alternative pressure generating mechanism is sought. To the authors knowledge limited optimi­
sation of vaneless pump geometry have been documented in literature. Topology optimisation on
a vaneless impeller for a swirl flow pump has has been studied by Alonso et al. in [2, 3, 4]. Sizing
optimisation of a shrouded Rayleigh step viscous pump was performed by Sato et al. [28] as well
as comparing the optimised pump to a conventional spiral groove viscous pump. The concept of
topology optimisation of impellers was verified by manufacturing and testing a optimised impeller
geometry by Sá et al. in [24].
Taking inspiration from hydrodynamic thrust bearings which have a time invariant pressure distri­
bution it is suggested to use shear driven flow over a stationary geometry with a outlet hole thus
generating a constant outlet pressure and volume flow. Figure 1.2 shows the intended operating
principle for a parallel step slider bearing which is similar to the operating principle examined by
Sato et al. in [28].
h0+sh
h0
ub
po,Qo
po
p
Figure 1.2: Simplified pressure distribution p and flow in cross section of parallel step pump ge­
ometry. Inspired by Sato [28].
In figure 1.2 the upper surface is moving at velocity ub which generates a shear stress driving the
flow. The height reduction of the shoulder height sh down to the minimum height h0 generates a
pressure distribution with a peak pressure at the step with shoulder height sh. The outlet is placed
on the stationary surface thus the pressure becomes time invariant above the outlet which leads
to constant outlet pressure po and flow rate Qo. The pressure distribution in hydrodynamic thrust
bearings is described by the Reynolds equation.
Due to the limited number of analytical solutions to Reynolds equation numerical methods are
introduced such as the finite difference method and finite element method as shown by Rohde
and McAllister [23], Santos et al. [26, 17, 25], Lengiewicz et al. [20] and Andreasen [5]. Tak­
ing inspiration from the numerical methods described in the mentioned literature a finite element
formulation is chosen for this thesis.
2
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:13==
Optimisation of the infinitely wide hydrodynamic thrust bearing was performed by Lord Rayleigh
in his ”Notes on the Theory of Lubrication”, [21] from 1918. In more recent time the optimisation
was extended to finite width hydrodynamic thrust bearings by Rohde and MacAllister [23] and
Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18]. This literature showed that the topography optimisation problem
for hydrodynamic thrust bearings was well posed thus this thesis aims at recreating the optimised
height distributions found in literature prior to expanding the usage of topography optimisation in
thin film problems by the introduction of a pump outlet orifice.
1.2
Definitions
The fluid film shown in figure 1.2 is contained between two plates. The surface shape of a plate is
called the topography and is determined by the local variation of the plate surface height. Topog­
raphy optimisation aims at determining the optimal distribution of surface height, topography of a
plate, for a given problem. Topography optimisation has seen widespread use in problems related
to metal sheet, Christensen and Klarbring [11].
Returning to the thin film fluid, the film height at any point between the plates is finite and non­
zero which means that the topography of the plate surface is directly coupled to the topography of
the film height.
Topology describes the shape of the fluid domain whereas topography is limited to describing the
film height. Letting the fluid height go to zero will change the fluid domain thus affecting the
topology of the fluid, however this is an unwanted effect in hydrodynamic thrust bearings as this
lead to plate­on­plate contact which drastically alters the behaviour of the bearing.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
3
==PAGE:14==
2
Theory
This chapter covers the necessary theory to describe and model the pressure distribution in a hy­
drodynamic thrust bearing with relevant assumptions as well as optimisation theory relevant for
conducting topography optimisation of the thin film domain.
2.1
Governing equations of thin film lubrication
In thin film fluid lubrication the flow is classed as a slow viscous motion in which pressure and
viscous terms dominate, Hamrock et al. [16]. The pressure distribution in the fluid film is de­
scribed by a differential equation known as Reynolds equation. The Reynolds equation describes
the pressure generated in a fluid between two surfaces undergoing constant shearing, which is a
simple method for generating vast pressure with minimal moving parts.
The governing equation is required to describe the fluid in the hydrodynamic thrust bearings and
viscous pumps therefor this section contains a description of Reynolds equation. Reynolds equa­
tion is derived from Navier­Stokes equations and the continuity equation. The derivation of the
general Reynolds equation is briefly described in this section. For a full derivation see section A.
Navier­Stokes equations for a incompressible fluid without body forces and with constant viscosity
and density in Cartesian coordinates and the continuity equation is the starting point and shown in
equations (2.1) to (2.4).

With absolute viscosity η, film height h = h(x, y), mean surface velocity ˜uT = {˜u, ˜v} and
pressure p = p(x, y). In equation (2.12) the first term in the parentheses describes Couette flow
and the second term describes Poiseuille flow. Couette flow is shear driven flow of a viscous
fluid between two tangential surfaces where one surface is stationary and the other is moving as
shown in figure 2.2. Poiseuille flow is a pressure induced flow betwenn two stationary surfaces as
shown in figure 2.2. Considering the pressure distribution and velocities of surface a and b in the
hydrodynamic thrust bearing the principle of superposition is employed as shown in figure 2.2 to
obtain the velocity profile between surface a and b.
Letting the reduced Reynolds equation govern a pressure distribution on domain Ωwith boundary
Γ as shown in figure 2.3a and 2.7 the boundary condition acting on pressure p at boundary Γ is
affected by the fluid surrounding the bearing domain as shown in figure 2.3b. From figure 2.3b it
is seen that the bearing domain is submerged in a fluid reservoir with far greater film height to the
point that is does not affect pressure generation however the reservoir is pressurised at pΓ.

2.1.1
Modified Reynolds Equation
To enable the modelling of a viscous pump Reynolds equation is modified to allow an external
source or sink. An external source or sink can be modeled in multiple ways for thin film lubrication
with the standard reduced form of the Reynolds equation. Figure 2.4 shows the addition of a orifice
to surface a both in the x­y and x­z planes.

One possibility is to extend the Reynolds equation by the addition of a out of plane injection veloc­
ity, vinj, as done by Santos and Russo in [26], Heinrichson et al. [17], Vásquez [33] and Salazar
and Santos [25]. Figure 2.5 shows the velocity profiles between surface a and b as well as the
injection velocity vinj through a circular orifice.

laminar flow in a axisymmetric pipe which leads to a velocity distribution in the orifice as:

This theory was originally developed for circular injection orifices acting as source terms in a
hybrid bearing but letting pinj →0 the pressure gradient ∂p/∂z changes sign thus becoming a
sink term which can be used to model a circular pump outlet.
It was suggested by Vásquez in [33] to extended the model to arbitrarily shaped orifices by the
addition of a correction factor Cd to (2.14) as:

These methods aims at a better approximation of the mass flow rate through orifices at the cost of
extending the governing equation. Drawing parallels to modelling of boundary conditions in struc­
tural mechanics, these methods can be seen as spring boundary conditions where small displace­
ments on the boundary are allowed which leads to improved accuracy for the total displacement
field and derivatives thereof. The orifice length lo is inversely related to the stiffness of a spring
as a short lo leads to a pressure closer to pinj. Figure 2.6a shows a potential pressure distribution
for a hydrodynamic thrust bearing with a orifice with pinj = 0 and lo ≫0.

Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:19==
Another possibility is to consider the orifice as a fixed pressure po and enforce it as a ordinary
boundary condition in the finite element method which leads to a pressure distribution as shown
in figure 2.6b.
The benefits of the fixed pressure is that the standard reduced form of the Reynolds equation can
be employed without modifications and the finite element model is also applicable without exten­
sions. However the fixed pressure leads to kinks in the pressure distribution at the circumference
of the orifice unlike smooth pressure distribution obtained with the modified Reynolds equation.
Examining the pressure distributions obtained with the modified Reynolds equation in Santos and
Russo [26] and Heinrichson et al. [17] it is seen that kinks also occur in the pressure distribution
which means the fixed pressure approach is viable.
As the project aims at developing the optimal pump geometry the orifice shape and location is of
interest thus the implementation should be easily adapted to design variables to be used within the
optimisation routine covered in section 2.3. In topology optimisation of fluid flow problems the
Brinkman friction term, α, is used as a penalisation of the velocities as done by Alexandersen in
[1].
Taking inspiration from Alexandersen [1] and the penalty functions to enforce constraints in finite
element methods from Cook et al. [12] it is suggested to add a penalisation as a large value of α to
nodes within the orifice and α = 0 outside the orifice. This is an unproven approach and as such
a section is dedicated to the derivation and implementation of the suggested method in section 2.2
as it is based on the system of equations obtained via the finite element formulation.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
9
==PAGE:20==
2.2
Finite element method
This section covers the derivation of the Galerkin finite element formulation employed in this thesis
to solve the Reynolds equation.
Andreasen [5] employed finite element method to sovle the Reynolds equation for both one and two
dimensional fluid domains. The finite element method was used to solve the Reynolds equation in a
finite width hydrodynamic thrust bearing by Rohde and McAllister [23]. The finite element method
was also used by Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18] to obtain the pressure distribution in finite width
bearing domains for various discretisations. The finite element method was used to solve a mixed
formulation of the mass conserving caviation model in Lengiewicz et al. [20]. de Kraker et al. [13]
used finite element methods to solve a texture averaged Reynolds equation. The Reynolds equation
was extended to include inertia terms and solved with a finite element method by Brunetière and
Tournerie in [8].
Rohde and McAllister [23] compared the finite element method with the finite difference method,
which is an alternative numerical method for solving the Reynolds equation. The finite difference
method has been used to examing the pressure distribution in journal bearings by Santos et al. [26,
17, 25].
The Galerkin finite element formulation employed in this thesis is primarily based on the work
performed by Lengiewicz et al. in [20].
2.2.1
Weak form of governing equation
The strong form of governing equation (2.12) consists of continuous functions describing the pres­
sure distribution p, film height h, mean surface velocities ˜u and fluid viscosity η that holds true
for any point in domain Ω. The weak form of governing equation only requires the functions of
the strong form holds true on integral form or average. The weak form is required for obtaining
the discretised equations.
The weak form of the governing equation is found by multiplying the strong form of the governing
equation, (2.12), by a test function, q, and integrating over the domain, Ω.

2.2.3
Penalty method for boundary conditions
As suggested in section 2.1.1 the penalty method for enforcing boundary conditions is required for
topology optimisation of plates containing the fluid film. In this section the penalty method for
enforcing boundary conditions in a finite element method is described.
The basic concept of the penalty method for enforcing boundary conditions is to introduce a penalty
for violation of prescribed boundary conditions. A prescribed pressure for the i’th node is intro­
duced in the i’th position of the prescribed pressure vector b and a penalty number αi ≥0 is
introduced in the diagonal matrix A. For αi = 0 the prescribed pressure is ignored while increas­
ing αi increases the penalty for violating the prescribed pressure at node i.
The benefits of this method is that prescribed pressure can be implemented with minimal impact
to bookkeeping and without affect the number of unknowns in the system of equations. However
the value of αi requires attention as larger values of αi can result in ill­conditioning of the global
system of equations and low values of αi fails to enforce the prescribed pressure of the i’th node.
2.2.4
Numerical integration
The integration of field quantities required to generate the element matrices for the global system
of equations are performed numerically. This section gives a brief introduction to the used methods
of numerical integration is this project.
The concept of numerical integration of a field quantity f(x, y) is to evaluate f(x, y) at specific
points, multiplying by a weighting factor and adding the results. Various methods of numerical
integration simply varies the specific locations for evaluation of f(x, y) and the values of the
weighting factors. Numerical integration in natural coordinates (¯x, ¯y) is expressed as:
Z 1
−1
Z 1
−1
f(¯x, ¯y) d¯x d¯y =
X
i
X
j
WiWjf(¯xi, ¯yj)
(2.35)
where Wi and Wj are the weighting factors for the i’th and j’th evaluation point. The numerical
integration of a linear function is exact for two evaluation points, thus four points are required
to integrate the field quantities in the bilinear element exactly. The classical method for finite
element formulation is Gaussian quadrature, Cook et al. [12], where table 2.1 shows the locations
and weighting factors required to obtain exact integration of the bilinear element.
It was shown by Lengiewicz et al. in [20] that the numerical integration had an effect on the
void fraction of a cavitation model for Reynolds equation. For Gaussian quadrature spikes in the
void fraction was observed. A similar observation was done by Andreasen in [5] for the pressure
distribution in a topography optimised hydrodynamic thrust bearing. Lengiewicz et al. showed that
a Lobatto quadrature reduced the spikes observed for the void fraction, thus a similar approach is
examined in this project to determine if the numerical integration is the cause of pressure spikes
seen by Andreasen. Table 2.1 shows the shows the locations and weighting factors for Lobatto
quadrature.

Lastly the concept of underintegration is described as it proved useful in section D for calculating

element domain Ωe, which is obtained by evaluating a single point, which leads to lower computa­
tional demand, but allows spurious modes in the solution. As the volume flow rates are derivatives
of the field quantities the spurious modes are unlikely to be activated. The location of the single
evaluation point is shown in table 2.1.

2.3
Topography Optimisation
This section covers a brief introduction to topography optimisation of the thin film problem and
how the thin film optimisation problem is solved. For a brief introduction to general optimisation
see appendix C or Klarbring and Christensen [11].
Optimisation of the hydrodynamic thrust bearing started with Lord Rayleigh determining the opti­
mal bearing geometry for the infinitely wide bearing in [21], which became known as the Rayleigh
step bearing. In 1955 Kettleborough proved experimentally that a shrouded step bearing provided
greater load capacity than a tilting­pad bearing, [19]. Optimisation through trial and error with
numerical models by Chow et al. in [10] showed that for certain configurations a combination of
shrouded step and incline proved greater than a shrouded step bearing.
A iterative optimisation algorithm for the finite width bearing under constant velocity and load
capacity was developed and employed by Rohde and McAllister in [23], which showed a opti­
mised shrouded step bearing with a load capacity improvement of 85% then compared to a fixed
incline bearing. Yoon and Choi extended the optimisation problem to account for stability in a air
lubricated slider bearing in [34] and [35].
The finite width bearing studied by Rohde and McAllister was recently re­visited by Kalliorinne
and Almqvist [18], where it is was shown that the optimised bearing geometry obtained by Rohde
and McAllister could be further improved. Further [18] optimised the finite width bearing geome­
try with respect to minimising drag and the coefficient of friction. Another example is Andreasen
[5], where the optimal bearing geometry for both the infinitely wide and finite width bearings were
examined.
The concept of using a hydrodynamic thrust bearing as a viscous pump was studied by Sato et al.
in [27] and [28] where basic sizing optimisation was used to determine the optimal geometry of a
viscous pump based on a shrouded Rayleigh step bearing.
2.3.1
Method of Moving Asymptotes
The Method of Moving Asymptotes, MMA, by Svanberg [31, 32], is an optimisation algorithm for
general constrained nonlinear problems. MMA is well suited for problems with large numbers of
design variables subject to few constraint equations which is a common problem within the field
of topology optimisation.
In short MMA solves nonlinear problems via a iterative process. Per iteration the nonlinear ob­
jective function is approximated by a strictly convex subproblem as shown in figure 2.11. The
subproblems are generated by moving­asymptotes, that are implemented to speed up and stabilise
convergence of the iterative process.
MMA has been applied with success to fluid based optimisation problems and fluid film lubrication
optimisation problems such as done by Alexandersen in [1] and by Andreasen in [5], thus it is
employed in this thesis.
2.3.2
Interpolation of film thickness
The film thickness h is defined as a function of the design variable γ via linear interpolation as
shown in (2.36) as done by Andreasen in [5]. This approach is chosen, as topography optimisation
within thin film theory is one of the few areas of optimisation where black and white designs are not
sought after, due to the well defined meaning of gray scales. In this project the linear interpolation
per nodal height hn is defined as:
hn(γn) = h0 + γn(hmax −h0)
(2.36)
For 0 ≤γn ≤1. The interpolation is shown in figure 2.12a for a set of h0 and hmax.

2.3.4
Adjoint Sensitivities
As MMA is a gradient­based optimisation algorithm the gradients for the objective function and
constraint equations with respect to the design variables are required. The gradients can be obtained
through analytical or numerical means as well as a combination thereof. Generally in the field
of topology optimisation the gradients are referred to as sensitivities, which is followed for the
following sections of this thesis.
The adjoint approach is commonly used to determine the sensitivities of objective functionals and
constraint equations within topology optimisation. The adjoint approach is computationally effi­
cient when the number of design variables exceeds the number of objective functionals, Giles and
Pierce [15]. This is generally the case for topology optimisation as the number of design variables
is related to the discretisation either by the number of elements or nodes in the finite element model.
The number of box constraints is equal to the number of design variables, but these constraints are
easily handled within most optimisation algorithms, Alexandersen [1].
According to Giles and Pierce in [15] the adjoint approach can be split into the discrete and con­
tinuous adjoint approach. Both approaches yield discrete adjoint equations however the path from
the partial differential equations, PDE, is different. The continuous approach consists of linearis­
ing the PDE, forming the adjoint problem and discretising the continuous adjoint problem. In the
discrete approach the PDE is discretised then linearised and the adjoint problem is formed.
The continuous adjoint problem retains a physical meaning at the cost of ”inexact” sensitivities
whereas the discrete adjoint approach retains ”exact” sensitivities, but lacks physical meaning.
Here the meaning of ”exact” and ”inexact” refers to the derivatives for the discretised system of
equations, not the continuous equations. Further the discrete adjoint problem ensures physically
feasible designs for each iteration whereas the continuous adjoint problem only ensures a physically
feasible design when the optimisation is converged.
In this thesis the discrete approach is chosen due to the physically feasible designs for each iteration.
Section 2.3.4 covers the derivation of the discrete adjoint problem and adjoint sensitivities required
for thin film optimisation.
Discrete adjoint sensitivities

necessary derivatives are described. Starting with the general objective functional:

2.3.5
Filtering
Several optimisation problems benefit from filtering of the design variables for various reasons.
Thin film problems where the objective is to maximize load capacity has been shown to be a well­
posed problem which does not need filtering, see for example the optimised results by Rohde and
McAllister [23], Buscaglia et al. [9] and Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18].
In structural optimisation several numerical instabilities such as checkerboard patterns, single node
pivots and mesh dependency exists, see Christensen and Klarbring [11]. The optimisation routine
typically finds and exploits assumptions and weakness in the numerical model to achieve greater
objective values.
In solid mechanics checkerboard patterns are densities oscillating between void and solid in neigh­
bouring elements which leads to elements connected by the corners as shown in figure 2.13. This
configuration is artificially stiff thus it is advantageous in minimising compliance and the pattern
covers large areas of the design domain. Similarly the single node pivot exploits the lack of rotation
in nodes to connect elements as shown in figure 2.13.
Figure 2.13: Numerical instabilities checkerboard pattern and single node pivot. Figure inspired
by Alexandersen [1].
The thin film problem has been shown to be mesh dependent by Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18]
via mesh refinement. This is an unwanted effect as mesh refinement should yield a converging
distribution of density not affecting the topography via a element related length scale. From figure
2.14 it is seen that the finer discretisation changes the topology of a half MBB beam by splitting
the diagonal bars in two then compared with the coarser discretisation.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
19
==PAGE:30==
(a) Design with 60x20 elements.
(b) Design with 120x40 elements.
Figure 2.14: Mesh dependent MBB designs generated with the 88 line MATLAB code for topology
optimisation from Andreassen [6].
To avoid these numerical instabilities, filtering of the design variables can be employed. Various
filters are investigated by Sigmund in [29], from which the basic density filtering is employed in
this project.
In solid mechanics the aim is to generate black and white designs as the physical interpretation of
0 > γ > 1 is difficult. In topography optimisation of film thickness the region 0 > γ > 1 is well
defined thus the requirement of filtering to generate black and white designs is non­existing. The
reason for selecting density filtering is to remove mesh dependency with minimal impact to the
design variable region 0 > γ > 1 and value of the objective functional.
Basic density filtering works by determining the film height of a node based on a function of design
variables of the neighboring nodes. The neighborhood of node n is specified by nodes within a
radius R of the node n:
Nn = {n | ∥xi −xn∥≤R}
(2.54)
where xi is the spatial location of node i and xn is the spatial location of node n. The filtered
density of the n’th node with neighborhood Nn is defined as:
˜γn =
X

X

(2.55)
where w(xi) is a weighting function and ai the area associated with the i’th node. The weighting
function is a linearly decaying cone shape function defined as:
w(xi) = R −∥xi −xn∥
(2.56)
where R is the filter radius. The area ai associated with the i’th node is required for correct filtering
of irregular discretisation, however the results covered in this thesis is limited to regular discreti­
sation, thus it is not required. As the optimisation routine updates the density γ but the design is
based on the now physically meaningful filtered density ˜γ the sensitivities are determined via the
chain rule as:

3
Results
This section focuses on the results obtained for the optimisation of viscous pump geometries. Prior
to introducing optimised pump geometries, the numerical implementations of the infinitely wide
and finite width bearings are examined. First the implementations are verified against known
analytical solutions for both infinitely wide and finite width bearing domains. Afterwards the op­
timised results and investigations are shown for various combinations of the objective functional
derived in appendix D. Lastly the viscous pump is optimised with topography and topology opti­
misation.
It is noted that all results shown in this chapter and relevant appendices shows the topography of
surface a and surface b moves from left to right.
3.1
Infinitely wide bearing
The infinitely wide bearing domain was optimised by Lord Rayleigh in [21] to achieve the highest
possible load capacity. Due to the existence of Lord Rayleigh’s solution and the simplicity of
infinitely wide bearing these are used to verify the finite element formulation and optimisation
routine described in chapter 2.
Figure 3.1 shows the layout and boundary conditions of the infinitely wide bearing problem. The
domain consists of a fluid film between the stationary upper surface a and the lower surface b
sliding at velocity ub. The pressure at the inlet and outlet regions pi and po are set to the ambient
pressure, which is chosen as p = 0. The entire bearing geometry is assumed submersed in fluid,
which leads to the inlet and outlet regions consisting of full lubrication fluid.

problem. Light grey denotes the design domain and hd denotes the design height.
The most common bearing design is a fixed­incline bearing, which consists of two flat plates with
a small angle between them as shown with a dashed line in figure 3.2. Lord Rayleigh, showed that
a parallel step geometry generated a higher load capacity. Figure 3.2 shows a parallel step bearing
as a dotted line. The optimal configuration of parallel step bearing was determined as (3.1) by Lord
Rayleigh. The optimal parallel step geometry as specified by (3.1) is referred to as a Rayleigh step
bearing.

3.1.1
Discretisation
Prior to conducting optimisation of the infinitely wide bearing domain the finite element formu­
lation is validated via convergence studies in appendix E.1. For a given bearing geometry the
number of regular elements was increased and the relative discrepancy between comparable ana­
lytical solutions and the numerically obtained pressure distribution as well as volume flow rates
was examined. Figure 3.3 shows the obtained convergence curves for a fixed­incline bearing ge­
ometry and a parallel­step bearing.
(a) Fixed­Incline slider bearing
(b) Parallel­Step slider bearing
Figure 3.3: Relative errors and run time for varying discretisation of infinitely wide bearing prob­
lem. Where p denotes the relative error of pressure, Qi and Qo denotes the relative errors of volume
flow rate and t denotes the run time.
From figure 3.3 it is seen that the pressure distribution and volume flow rates of the inlet and outlet
are converging for increasing number of elements until an element length on order of magnitude
E = 10−5 m is reached, at which point cancellation errors are observed. A discrepancy between
inlet and outlet volume flow rates is seen in figure 3.3a for the fixed­incline bearing whereas no
discrepancy is seen for the parallel­step bearing in figure 3.3b. The discrepancy is discussed in
section 4.1.1. As is common with numerical methods the run time is observed to be inversely
related to the element length E for both bearing domains.

3.1.2
Maximising load capacity
This section presents the results obtained by maximising the load capacity of a infinitely wide
bearing domain with the optimisation routine described in chapter 2. The primary purpose of these
results is to compare the used optimisation routine to the well known optimal bearing geometry,
that is a Rayleigh step bearing. A similar study was performed by Andreasen in [5], which showed
agreement between the Rayleigh step bearing and a bearing geometry obtained numerically with a
optimisation routine.
The optimisation routine is allowed to vary the film height between a minimum h0 and a maximum
defined as h0 + hd, where hd is referred to as the design height and is shown in figure 3.1. The
optimisation routine is used for a bearing domain as defined in table 3.1 with boundary conditions
defined in table 3.2. The initial bearing geometry is a fixed­incline bearing with shoulder height
hd.

3.1.3
Minimising coefficient of friction
As mentioned by Lord Rayleigh [21] the Rayleigh step bearing increases both load capacity and
drag acting on the bearing surface. With the verified finite element formulation and optimisation
routine an alternative optimisation problem is proposed as minimising the coefficient of friction
for the hydrodynamic thrust bearing.
Examining the same infinitely wide bearing as described in section 3.1.2 the coefficient of friction
µ is minimised, which obtains the bearing design and pressure distribution shown in figure 3.5. The
initial bearing design is a fixed­incline as described in section 3.1.2. In this section the coefficient
of friction is defined as shear force over load capacity, where the shear force is obtained by the
fluid moving over surface a. Two optimisation problems are examined. A unconstrained optimi­
sation problem where the coefficient of friction is minimised while subject to box constraints and
a constrained optimisation problem where the coefficient of friction is minimised while subject to
inequality and box constraints.
(a) Optimised bearing geometries.
(b) Pressure distributions.
Figure 3.5: Comparison of optimised bearing configurations for fluid film thickness h and dimen­
sionless pressure distribution P. Unconstrained and constrained refers optimisation without and
with load capacity inequality constraint. Analytical refers to the Rayleigh step bearing.
From figure 3.5a it is seen that the optimised bearing for both unconstrained and constrained has
a larger shoulder height sh and the step is replaced by a steep incline with smoothed transition
from inlet plateau and outlet plateau. This bearing design leads to pressure distribution as shown
in figure 3.5b, which shows a shallower pressure gradient up to a smoothed pressure peak and a
steeper gradient after the pressure peak. This pressure distribution reduces drag as described in
appendix E.3.
Figure 3.5 contains two optimised geometries, that are both obtained by minimising the coefficient
of friction. The geometry labeled Unconstrained is obtained with a optimisation problem similar to
(3.2)­(3.4), whereas the Constrained geometry is the result of a constrained optimisation problem
defined as:
min

The introduction of a maximum load capacity Φ∗

referring to a unconstrained problem it is a optimisation problem subject to only box constraints
while a constrained problem is subject to both box and inequality constraints. In appendix E.3 it
was shown that the optimised bearing geometries reduced the coefficient of friction by 17.85%
for the unconstrained problem and 8.84% for the constrained problem from the initial value of
µ = 0.0049. Comparing the unconstrained coefficient of friction µ = 0.0040 with the coefficient
of friction for the Rayleigh step µ = 0.0041 it is seen that the Rayleigh step is close to optimal
bearing geometry when considering the coefficient of friction.

==PAGE:38==
3.2
Finite width bearing
The finite width bearing problem described in this section is compared to analytical solutions for
the infinitely wide bearing and finite width bearing from Hamrock [16], as well as numerical work
by Rohde and McAllister in [23], Buscaglia et al. in [9], Andreasen in [5] and Kalliorinne and
Almqvist in [18].
Figure 3.6 shows the layout and boundary conditions of the finite width bearing problem. The
domain consists of a fluid film between the stationary upper surface a and the lower surface b
sliding at velocity ub. The pressure at the inlet and outlet regions, pi and po, are set to the ambient
pressure. The outlet region for the pressure boundary condition is assumed to include both sides
and the rear of the domain. The entire bearing geometry is assumed submersed in fluid which leads
to the inlet and outlet regions consisting of full lubrication fluid. The modified Reynolds number
along the x direction is defined by the bearing length l, sliding velocity ub, the minimum fluid
film height h0, and the fluid density ρ and viscosity η. The modified Reynolds number along the y
direction is defined by the bearing width b, sliding velocity vb, the minimum fluid film height h0,
and the fluid density ρ and viscosity η.
The design domain for the optimisation routine is limited to the stationary surface a with an al­
lowable range of film height as h ∈[h0; hmax] with hmax = hd + h0. As the fluid film height
is implemented with linear elements the height field is C0 continuous which also holds for the
optimised height distribution.
z
x
l
ub
pi
hd
h0
po
y
b
Figure 3.6: Schematic illustration of sizes and boundary conditions for the finite width bearing
problem. Light grey denotes the design domain and hd denotes the design height.
The finite element method is compared to a analytical pressure distribution in appendix F.1 and a
discretisation study is performed in appendix F.1.1. These appendices shows convergence of the
finite element method and agreement between analytical and numerical pressure distributions for
finite width bearings.

Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings

Maximising load capacity
This section shows the results for maximising load capacity in a finite width bearing domain.

Considering a bearing domain as shown in figure 3.6 with geometric and fluid properties from table

bearing as seen in figure 3.7a.

From figure 3.7a it is seen that the design height hd is not fully utilised which indicates that a
optimal dimensionless film thickness H0 = h0/sh exists. Another interesting observation from
the optimised bearing design is a series of vanes occurring at the transition from shroud to step.
These vanes serves to reduce side leakage due to a y­direction pressure gradient. This effect is
observed in the pressure distribution of figure 3.7b, where the internal corners of the pressure
contours show the widened high pressure zone due the additions of vanes.
The optimised bearing shown in figure 3.7 is obtained by solving the optimisation problem defined
in (3.2)­(3.4). In appendix F.2 a parameter study is conducted for various length­to­width ratios

determine if an optimal bearing design for a given length­to­width ratio exists. Figure 3.8 shows

curves are discussed further in section 4.2 and appendix F.2.

pump.

3.3
Viscous pump optimisation
The introduction of a pump orifice in the finite width bearing domain is examined in this section.
Three approaches for optimising the viscous pump based hydrodynamic thrust bearings are exam­
ined.
The first method is the introduction of various orifices in the finite width bearing subject to a pre­
viously used optimisation problem.
The second method is a volume flow rate optimisation problem for the finite width bearing domain
with various orifices.
Lastly a topology optimisation of the plates containing the thin film is conducted. Figure 3.6 shows
the layout and boundary conditions of the finite width bearing problem. The design domain for the
optimisation routine is limited as described in section 3.2.

3.3.1
Simple pump optimisation
This section covers optimised results obtained for a finite width bearing domain where the intro­
duction of a pump orifice leads to a volume flow rate through the orifice, which is of interest as
it is a measure of pump efficiency. Results of interest from appendix G.1 is shown in this section
and discussed in section 4.3. Figure 3.16 shows the optimised bearing designs for the three orifices
described in table 3.6.
Figure 3.16: Optimised finite width bearing geometries wrt. Φload for H0 = 1.2. Color represents
the design variable γ where the physical film thickness is h = h0 + γhd. Blue circle marks edge
of orifice.
From figure 3.16 it is observed that qualitatively the optimised bearing designs attempts to surround
the orifices with a region of film height h0, which is an effort to minimise the impact of the enforced
pressure of po = 0 on pressure distributions, shown in figure 3.17. Generally the optimised bearing
designs are best described as modified shrouded step bearings, which is expected as it is the same
optimisation problem used to generate these bearing geometries as for the full domains described
in section 3.2.1. Vanes similar to those observed in section 3.2.1 are present in one of the optimised
bearing geometries seen in figure 3.7a.
Figure 3.17: Normalised pressure distribution for optimised finite width bearing geometries with
H0 = 1.2. Color represents normalised pressure p/ max(p). Blue circle marks edge of orifice.
Letting Qi denote the volume flow rate into bearing domain Ωand Qo denote volume flow rate
through the pump orifice the pump efficiency can be described as the fraction Qo/Qi. Introducing
the orifices described in table 3.6 in a finite width Rayleigh step bearing leads to the following
values of volume flow rate fractions:

3.3.3
Pump topology optimisation
In this section the optimised results for maximising the volume flow rate through a pump orifice
in a finite width bearing geometry is examined. A fixed surface topography is introduced and the
topology of the containing plate is optimised. The surface topography is a fixed incline slider with
length­to­width ratio λ = 1 and dimensionless film thickness H0 = 1.
As described in section 2.3.3 the orifice pressure po = 0 is enforced by a penalty method, where
the design variable ξn controls the penalty factor αn for the n’th node. Figure 3.25 shows the effect
of varying the maximum penalisation parameter αmax.
Figure 3.25: Optimised finite width bearing plate a. Color represents the design variable ξ where
the penalty value is α = αmin + ξαmax. The orifice is represented as ξ = 1.
From figure 3.25 it is seen that the effect of αmax drastically changes the topology of bearing plate a.
Comparing the orifice marked by ξ = 1 in figure 3.25 with the corresponding pressure distributions
in figure 3.26 it is seen that for low values of αmax the orifice is located at the peak pressure. For
high values of αmax the orifice is located at the boundary Γ, where the boundary conditions of
pΓ = 0 is enforced. For intermediate values of αmax ring­like structures are observed, which can
be related to a equivalent pressure as seen in figure 3.26.
The values for αmax were chosen empirically to ensure that the lowest value had close to zero
effect on the pressure distribution while the largest value enforced the orifice pressure po = 0.
This selection leads to αmax = 10−6 representing a weak coupled system, where the pressure
distribution has a large impact on the orifice distribution. For αmax = 102 a strong coupling is
observed.
42
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:53==
For large values of αmax, ξ = 1 indicates a hole, while 0 < ξ < 1 is considered a porous plate.
For smaller values of αmax all values of ξ ̸= 0 are considered a porous plate.
The effect of changing αmax on the volume flow rate through the orifice is shown in figure 3.27. It
is observed that for increasing αmax the volume flow rate through the orifice Qo converges toward
Qo = 615.2 m3 s−1. Examining the orifice distribution in figure 3.25 and the pressure distributions
in figure 3.26 a converging design is seen.
Figure 3.26: Dimensionless pressure for optimised plate topology with unfiltered design variables
ξ. Color represents dimensionless pressure.
Letting αmin = 0 and αmax ∈{10−6, 102} with a penalty factor of k = 1000 the volume flow
rates for the optimised bearing geometries are shown in figure 3.27, where the results are obtained
without continuation approach.The continuation approached yielded the same optimised designs
as seen in figure 3.25 for the chosen maximum value for αmax, thus the results are not shown.
Figure 3.27 shows the volume flow rate Qo for both a unfiltered and filtered design. The filtered
designs are shown in appendix G.4.

4
Discussion
In this section the optimised results from chapter 3 are discussed. The discussion is split into
separate sections covering each section of the optimised results.
4.1
Infinitely wide bearing
In this section the results obtained for the infinitely wide bearing domain is discussed. Where
possible comparisons to similar results in literature is conducted.
4.1.1
Discretisation
The convergence studies performed in appendix D showed the convergence rates for the field quan­
tity and its derivatives ­ that is the pressure p and volume flow rates Qi and Qo. For a fixed incline
bearing the pressure showed greater convergence than the volume flow rates. This is expected for
finite element formulations as the volume flow rates depend on the gradient of the pressure field,
see Cook et al. [12]. An unexpected behaviour was seen for the parallel­step bearing where the vol­
ume flow rates have the same convergence rate as the pressure. It is stipulated, that this is caused
by the simple pressure distribution consisting of two regions with constant pressure gradient. The
discrepancy observed between inlet and outlet volume flow rates for the fixed­inclined bearing is
expected, as Reynolds equation is not mass conserving. However as was shown in appendix D,
and by Brunetière and Tournerie in [8], the effect becomes negligible for finer discretisation.
4.1.2
Maximising load capacity
The infinitely wide bearing geometries obtained from solving the optimisation problem are similar
to the Rayleigh step bearing, which indicates that the optimisation routine reaches the optimal
configuration as derived by Lord Rayleigh [21]. The slight discrepancy observed between the
two are expected to be caused by a combination of discretisation and order of magnitudes for the
sensitivities of Φload. The Rayleigh step consist of two plateaus connected by a step, whereas
the finite element formulation connects nodes with a linear function, that among other variables
represents the film height h, which leads to the step being represented by a steep incline.
Another cause is related to the small effect of altering the film height, just in front of the step,
which is seen as fluctuations of the film height in the close up of figure 3.4a. It is expected that the
small order of magnitudes for the sensitivities of Φload is the primary cause of the discrepancies of
film height distribution at the step in figure 3.4a.
In appendix E.2 it was shown that fluid properties and surface velocity ub show no effect on the op­
timised bearing geometry. It was suggested that varying the dimensionless film thickness H0 have
an effect on the optimised bearing geometry as H0 < 1.155 the optimal geometry is unobtainable,
thus an alternative geometry is reached.
4.1.3
Minimising coefficient of friction
The unconstrained minimisation problem of the coefficient of friction in a infinitely wide bearing
domain showed a geometry with a inclined step, that had a reduction of 17.85% from the initial
fixed­incline bearing geometry. The constrained problem lead to a lower reduction of 8.84% which
was shown in appendix E.3 to be caused by the constant load. Examining the load capacity and
drag functions:
Φload =
Z
Ω
p dΩ
and
Φdrag = −h
2∇p −ηu
h
(4.1)
it is seen that the load leads to increasing pressure, thus the pressure distribution is similar to the
pressure distribution of the Rayleigh step bearing. The shallower pressure gradient in the inlet
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
45
==PAGE:56==
region is caused by the definition of drag, where the term ∇p is present, thus reducing ∇p leads to
less drag due to reduced adverse pressure gradient. Similarly for the outlet region, increasing the
pressure gradient leads to a larger favorable pressure gradient. The smooth pressure peak occurs as
maximising pressure, while showing a larger region of ∇p ≈0 leads to a less drag in the location
of maximum pressure.
As shown in figure 3.5a, the film height distribution near the pressure peak for the constrained
optimised bearing design shows fluctuations, that are expected to widen the region of ∇p ≈0,
which increases load capacity with minimal impact to drag. In appendix E.3 it is suggested to
maintain a minimum load capacity while minimising drag to avoid local film height fluctuations
in the optimised bearing design.
4.2
Finite width bearing
In this section the results obtained for the finite width bearing domain is discussed, and where
possible comparisons to similar results in literature are conducted.
4.2.1
Discretisation
The convergence studies performed in appendix F.1.1 showed the convergence rates for the field
quantity and its derivatives, that is the pressure p and volume flow rate Q across bearing boundary
Q. As for the infinitely wide parallel step bearing the convergence rates of p and Q are identical
for the finite width parallel step bearing.
The comparison study in appendix F.1 showed agreement between the analytical and numerical
pressure distributions in a finite width parallel step bearing. It was seen that the numerical pres­
sure distributions have a minor overestimation for both Gaussian and Lobatto integration. The
convergence study of relative improvement of load capacity in a finite width bearing in appendix
F.1.1 showed the relative improvement converging for increasing number of elements until a mesh
with 130 × 130 elements. For finer discretisations the relative improvement fluctuates, which can
indicate mesh dependencies. This is supported by section F.2 in appendix F.2 where the presence
of vanes in the optimised bearing design begins for a discretisation of 128 × 128 elements.
Combining the negligible differences between pressure distributions and optimised bearing designs
for Gaussian and Lobatto numerical integration methods it appears to have no noticeable effects
on the optimised bearing geometries.
4.2.2
Maximising load capacity
The optimised finite width bearing designs can qualitatively be described as shrouded step bearings
as seen in section 3.2.1 and appendix F.2. From the curves of relative improvement of load capacity
it can be seen, that the load capacity converges for decreasing H0 = h0/hd. This indicates that
a optimal shoulder height sh exists for each length­to­width ratio λ. The optimal dimensionless
film thickness is estimated as H0 ≈0.9 for λ = 0.5 and H0 ≈0.75 for λ = 1. Since the relative
improvement for λ = 2 is not converged within the examined interval, it is estimated as H0 ≈0.3.
It appears that the optimal H0 approaches H0 = 1.155 for decreasing length­to­width ratio λ. This
is expected as the infinitely wide bearing for which Lord Rayleigh determined the optimal bearing
geometry as H0 = 1.155 with step location ns = 0.7182 can be described as having λ = 0.
The optimised bearing design shown in figure 3.7 is similar to the shrouded step bearings obtained
by Rohde and McAllister [23] and Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18]. The vanes observed in figure 3.7
appears for low values H0 and λ and are expected to widen the high pressure zone at the bearing
step while minimising side leakage due to the high pressure zone. A similar conclusion was reached
by Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18]. The combination of fine points at both ends of the vanes and
the full utilisation for design height hd at the vanes indicates that these are a direct result of mesh
dependency, which is in agreement with the findings of the discretisation study by Kalliorinne and
46
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:57==
Almqvist [18]. The introduction of a density filter is expected to reduce mesh dependency, which
is indicated by the reduced load capacity seen in figure 3.8. However the near discrete step from
inlet plateau to outlet plateau and shroud observed in the optimised bearing designs is expected to
have a negative impact from filtering due to the averaging effect.
The filter radius should be kept small to avoid impacting the optimised bearing designs’ step, but
large enough to avoid mesh dependencies such as the vanes observed in figure 3.7. It is especially
interesting as the intermediate values 0 < γ < 1 are easily related to physically values, as the film
height h can fluctuate locally within the design height hd.
For a finite width bearing domain with length­to­width ratio λ = 1 and dimensionless film thick­
ness H0 = 0.15 the maximised dimensionless load capacity was determined as 0.1266. Rohde and
McAllister [23] found the maximum dimensionless load capacity as 0.1260749. This indicates that
the optimised bearing design is in agreement with the results obtained by Rohde and McAllister.
4.2.3
Minimising drag
The drag minimising finite width bearing designs shown in section 3.2.2 and appendix F.3 consists
of a shrouded inlet plateau with a small incline at the outlet. The rear incline acts as the pressure
generating mechanism. The narrow shrouds minimises side leakages which aids in improving
the pressure generation, as described in appendix F.2. The rear corners are elevated to maximum
allowable height which is explained by examining the definition of the drag objective functional
restated here:
Φdrag = −h
2∇p −ηu
h
(4.2)
Examining the pressure distributions for H0 = 0.6 and H0 = 1.05 in figure F.11 it is observed that
the pressure distribution in the rear corners is near zero, as is the pressure gradient ∇p. From this
and (4.2) it can be seen that the Poiseuille related term of the drag functional is close to zero as it
contains the pressure gradient ∇p, while the Couette related term contains the local film height h
in the denominator, which leads to maximising h minimises drag in the rear corners.
From figure 3.10 it is observed that for decreasing dimensionless film thickness H0, the relative
improvement increases for all examined length­to­width ratios λ in a similar manner. It appears
that the relative improvement is diverging for decreasing dimensionless film height H0, which
requires further examination of the optimised bearing geometry to determine if the optimisation
algorithm exploits the assumptions and weakness in the finite element formulation of thin film.
The intermediate plateau observed in figure 3.9 for unfiltered design variable γ and H0 = 0.15 is
expected to be caused by the same effect as the elevated reward corners.At the pressure peak in the
region of the intermediate plateau the pressure gradient ∇p ≈0, which is similar to region in the
rearward corners. The intermediate plateau is not observed for higher values of H0 as the design
height hd is fully utilised. The existence of the intermediate plateau shows, that the suggestion from
appendix E.3 was wrong, as both minimising drag and coefficient of friction subject to constant
load capacity yields local fluctuations at the pressure peak.
4.2.4
Minimising coefficient of friction
The results shown in section 3.2.3 and appendix F.4 for the minimisation of the coefficient of
friction µ share similarities with the optimised infinitely wide bearings from section 4.1.3: the
designs are qualitatively described as a parallel step bearing with a inclined step. This is similar to
the infinitely wide bearings. Further the shrouds observed for maximising load capacity are also
present for the optimised bearings in section 3.2.3 as these improve load capacity with minimal
increase of drag.
The vanes observed in section 3.2.1 are not present in the optimised bearing designs for minimum µ,
which indicates that the additional load capacity obtained with vanes is lower than the additional
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
47
==PAGE:58==
drag. The elevated rearward corners observed in section 3.2.2 are also present in the optimised
bearing designs for minimum µ. In appendix F.4 it was shown that the discrete increase of film
height led to negative pressures.
Figure 3.13 showed the intermediate plateau observed for low values of H0 in both drag and co­
efficient of friction optimisation problems. The pressure gradient along the x­direction shows the
region of nearly zero pressure gradient ∇p ≈0 coinciding with the intermediate plateau for both
unfiltered design variable γ and filtered design variable ˜γ. It was shown for minimising drag, that
a density filter changed the optimised topography to not include a intermediate plateau, but for
minimising the coefficient of friction the intermediate plateau remained. It is expected, that the
higher load capacity of the minimised µ results in the added benefits of the intermediate plateau
outweighing the drawbacks. As mentioned in section 4.2.3 the intermediate plateau leads to a
region of high pressure and low drag, thus improving the coefficient of friction.
Comparing the convergence behavior of load capacity, drag and coefficient of friction parameter
studies it is seen that the convergence of coefficient of friction is closer to that of the converging
load capacity than the diverging drag reductions. This behavior is expected, as maximising load
capacity while maintaining low drag leads to a lower coefficient of friction than pursuing large
reductions of drag. This is supported by comparing the Rayleigh step bearing with the optimised
bearing for unconstrained minimisation of coefficient of friction in the infinitely wide bearing
domain.
Returning to Reynolds equation it is noteworthy that discrete changes of film height is not ac­
counted for. This leads to bearing designs with low values of H0 = h0/sh and near discrete film
height changes be examined further to determine the physical feasibility as the assumptions of
the Reynolds equation is not kept. In the region of the intermediate plateau it is expected that a
swirl could form, thus leading to velocities in all Cartesian direction being on the same order of
magnitude.
For a finite width bearing domain with length­to­width ratio λ = 1 and dimensionless film thick­
ness H0 = 0.15 the minimised dimensionless coefficient of friction was determined as 5.9987.
Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18] found the minimum dimensionless coefficient of friction as 5.6350.
This indicates that the optimised bearing design is less efficient than that obtained by Kalliorinne
and Almqvist. It is expected that the higher allowable design height hd in [18] is the cause of the
discrepancy.
48
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:59==
4.3
Viscous pump optimisation
In this section the optimised results for the finite width viscous pump optimisation problem is
discussed.
4.3.1
Simple pump optimisation
This section covers the results obtained in appendix G.1 and shown in section 3.3.1. In figure 3.16
it was shown that the orifice location has a large impact on the optimised bearing topography.
Examining the optimised geometry for the centered circular orifice, marker M, in figure 3.16 it is
seen, that a shrouded parallel step bearing geometry is present with an island of height h0 around
the orifice. No vanes in the transition from shroud to step is observed, which was unexpected as
these occurred for the full domain in section 4.2.2. For larger values of H0 two large vanes are
observed for the island. The two vanes limits fluid flow from the sides towards the lower pressure
region behind the orifice. As the vanes becomes more prominent with increasing H0 the effect
is clearly visible in the pressure distributions in appendix G.1. A similar set of island vanes was
observed by Buscaglia et al. in [9] for a square orifice in the finite width bearing domain.
Examining the optimised design and pressure distribution for the rearward located orifice, marker
R, it is seen, that the rearward location of the orifice is near the location of the discrete step between
the inlet and outlet plateaus. The orifice location affects the shape of the step in the x­y plane as
seen in figure 3.16. Qualitatively the step geometry can be described as a forward bulge with
a bell shaped region of elevated film height behind the orifice. For increasing values of H0 the
bulge section of the step takes up a larger fraction of the total width of the step and for H0 = 2 a
series of small vanes are observed in appendix G.1. These vanes are oriented perpendicular to the
pressure contours from figure 3.17, which leads to the vanes being angled at ∼30°to the direction
of movement for surface b. The bell shape behind the orifice is used for letting fluid flow in behind
the orifice to allow pressure generation at the step from bell to outlet plateau, which is seen as small
strip of higher pressure at the rear edge of the bell in figure 3.17.
From appendix G.1 it is seen that the optimised bearing designs with a asymmetric located orifice,
marker S, shows significantly different topography for the examined values of H0. For lower
values of H0 the optimised bearing designs resemble those obtained for a rearward located orifice
with a bell behind the orifice. For lower values of H0 the bearing domain is essentially split into
two bearing domains with larger effective length­to­width ratios as described in appendix G.1. The
pressure distribution in figure 3.17 shows, that within the bell a typical pressure distribution of a
small shrouded step bearing exists. On a larger scale it is observed that the close proximity of the
orifice and outer edge leads to a large zone of nearly zero pressure between the orifice and edge
thus only three quarters of the bearing width b is able to use the total domain length l to generate
a large pressure peak at the discrete step.
As the primary reason to introduce orifices in the finite width bearing domain is to create a viscous
pump, the volume flow rate through the orifice was examined in appendix G.1. By comparing the
volume flow rate into the bearing domain with the volume flow rate through the orifice, figure
3.18a showed that for decreasing values of H0 the amount of fluid going to the orifice increases.
The rearward orifice location marked by R yielded the lowest volume flow rate fraction across
all H0 examined, which is expected to be caused by peak pressure in front of the orifice. This is
supported by the pressure distributions in figure 3.17 from which it is observed that the pressure
in front of the orifice is highest for the rearward located orifice. The asymmetric located orifice,
S, showed a larger volume flow rate fraction across all examined values of H0, which is further
supported by the lower pressure in front of the orifice marked by S.
In figure 3.18b the volume flow rate fraction for the optimised bearing designs were compared
to those obtained for a finite width Rayleigh step bearing with identical orifices. It was seen that
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
49
==PAGE:60==
maximising load capacity had a negative effect on the volume flow rate fraction. The reduction was
expected, as the introduction of a orifice reduces the load capacity, thus maximising load capacity
would ideally close the orifice. The closing of the orifice is seen as the islands surrounding the
orifices in figure 3.16.
4.3.2
Improved pump optimisation
This section covers the results obtained in appendix G.3 and shown in section 3.3.2. Due to the
reduction of the volume flow rate fraction for the results in section 3.3.1, an alternative optimisation
problem was utilised in appendix G.3 to obtain better viscous pump designs.
From figure 3.20 it is seen, that the optimised bearing designs resembles a shrouded step bearing.
Compared to the optimised design from section 3.16 it is observed, that the surrounding island
is drastically reduced for the circular orifices. For the introduction of high aspect square orifices
the surrounding islands of film height h0 remains, which indicates that the required load capacity
dominates the bearing design. The optimised design for circular orifices marked by M and S are of
particular interest as the front is open to the inlet plateau. The open orifices are expected to have a
larger volume flow rate due to the low pressure in front of them as seen in figure 3.21.
Examining the pressure distributions in figure 3.21 it is seen that the circular orifices marked by M
and S have minimal pressure in front of the orifices which leads to a higher volume flow rate into
the inlet plateau. Further the higher pressure surrounding the orifices guides more fluid towards
the orifices. For the other orifices the pressure in front of the orifices reduces the volume flow rate
in to the orifices. This is particularly easy to see for the wide square marked by Sq.W in figure
3.21, where a high pressure zone is present in front of the orifice. The high pressure pushes fluid
towards the edges instead of letting it flow in to the orifice.
The volume flow rate fraction for dimensionless film thickness H0 ∈{0.15, 2.1} is shown in
figure 3.22a, where it is seen, that the volume flow rate fraction Qo/Qi behaves similar for all
examined orifices. For lower values of H0 the volume flow rate fraction Qo/Qi appears to rapidly
approach unity. The extremely high volume flow rate fractions for low values of H0 are expected
to be the result of three effects:
The volume flow rate Qi is limited to the volume flow rate in through the inlet side, whereas the
actual volume flow rate into the bearing domain also includes potential suction occurring on the
sides.
The physical feasibility of the Reynolds equation for large shoulder heights sh is questionable due
to the possibility of swirls developing in front of the step.
Lastly the Reynolds equation is not mass conserving, which is especially relevant for coarser dis­
cretisation. Comparing the optimised designs in appendix G.3 it is suggested to implement a finer
discretisation as the bearing designs shows limited signs of suction along the sides. Further the
designs do not appear to redirect all fluid towards the orifices, which indicates that the lack of
mass conservation is the cause of large volume flow rate fractions.
The relative improvement of volume flow fraction Qo/Qi in figure 3.22b shows, that the centered
orifice M achieves a significantly higher improvement than the other examined orifices. For all
examined optimised pump designs a diverging improvement for low values of H0 is observed. For
H0 > 0.9 it is seen, that the square orifices generates a lower volume flow rate fraction Qo/Qi
in the optimised designs than in Rayleigh step designs. This indicates that the square orifices are
ill­suited as pump orifices, which is supported by the streamlines in figure 3.23.
In figure 3.24 the result of a simple sizing study was shown. For a single bearing domain with
λ = 1 and H0 = 1 the effect of the orifice diameter do of a centered circular orifice indicates the
existence of a optimal orifice size. Due to the existence of a optimal orifice diameter it is expected
that sizing optimisation of orifices could further improve the volume flow rate fraction.
50
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:61==
Appendix G.2 determined that the nodal consistent method of numerical integration led to poor
optimised pump designs. This is due to exploitation of a combination of boundary conditions and
Reynolds equation. Negative pressure was used to generate suction on the boundaries, which led
to nonphysical designs and large volume flow rates. This is further discussed in appendix G.2. The
solution found in appendix G.2 was the introduction of under integration. Under integration of the
volume flow rate drastically reduces the exploitation of boundary conditions. This led to reduced
negative pressure since employing the orifice led to higher volume flow rates.
4.3.3
Pump topology optimisation
This section covers the results obtained in section 3.3.3. The effect of varying the penalisation
number was examined in a finite width bearing domain.
Comparing the pressure distribution with penalisation parameter in figure 3.26 the value of αmax
can be considered as a coupling parameter, where a low value of αmax leads to a weak coupling.
The weak coupling is seen as the pressure distribution affects the location of the orifice, while the
orifice has minimal impact on the pressure distribution. For αmax = 10−6 it is seen, as the orifice is
located at the maximum pressure without decreasing the pressure whereas for αmax = the orifices
shows reduced pressure which indicates a strong coupling. As the coupling becomes strong, it
is noteworthy to return to the velocity profiles described in section 2.1, as the volume flow rate
through the inlet dictates the maximum volume flow rate obtainable in the bearing domain.
Since the film height h is kept as a fixed incline, the velocity profile between surface a and b consists
of a Couette term and a Poiseuille term from the adverse pressure gradient, thus introducing a
orifice, with strong coupling to the pressure distribution along the inlet, leads to pure Couette flow
into the inlet. The orifice enforces a pressure of p = 0, which allows the inlet to be pure Couette,
that is greater than the combined Couette and Poiseille flow. Similarly an orifice along the sides
reduces side leakages as this a purely pressure driven flow.
The fluctuations between 1 and 0 for the unfiltered design variables ξ leads to uneven pressure
distribution which is most noticeable for αmax = 10−5 and αmax = 10−1. The pressure distribu­
tions for αmax = 10−6 and αmax ≥100 are smooth. Despite the uneven pressure distributions it
is observed from figure 3.26 that the transitional values of αmax yields the highest maximum pres­
sure, which is expected due to the coupling being strong enough to move the orifices away from
the pressure peak but weak enough to not enforce a pressure of p = 0 at ξ = 1.
Introducing a density filter on the design variables is expected to have the greatest effect for the
transitional values of αmax as the weak and strong coupling lead to optimised designs without local
fluctuations of design variables.
From figure G.16 a similar relationship between optimised topology and value of αmax is observed
for the filtered design variables ˜ξ. For lower values of αmax a combination of using symmetry
to reduce the computational domain and filtering introduces a center line of solid, that effects the
topology. For αmax = 10−6 the center line effect is the only change to the optimised topology as
expected due the lack of small features in the unfiltered topology.
For large values of αmax the optimised topology remains largely unchanged as expected for the thin
strip of solid at the inlet observed in figure 3.25. The thin strip is not present in figure G.16 due
to filtering. The effect of filtering is most prominent in the optimised topology for the transitional
values of αmax.
The narrow rings seen in the unfiltered design for transitional values of αmax from figure 3.25 are
replaced by a few larger regions of ˜ξ = 1. For αmax = 10−5 and αmax = 10−4 the orifices are
split into a frontal row and a rear row which leads to a relatively low pressure around the boundary
Γ while maintaining a large region of high pressure contained by the orifices as seen in figure
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
51
==PAGE:62==
G.17. For αmax = 10−3 the ring shaped orifice reappears with a single ring and two small circular
orifices, which encircles the high pressure zone in the bearing thus maximising volume flow rate in
to the bearing domain Ω. For αmax = 10−2 a complete circle is seen, that acts in a similar manner
as the topology for αmax = 10−3. At a value of αmax = 10−1 the qualitative topology remains
unaltered by the introduction of filtering.
The topography optimisation of the pump domain yielded physically feasible designs. These de­
signs showed improved volume flow rate fractions Qo/Qi. Further the simple sizing study of the
orifice diameter showed that a optimal diameter existed. This indicated that sizing optimisation of
orifices could lead to improved pump efficiency. Instead of size optimisation, topology optimisa­
tion was implemented. Due to the topology optimised orifice being located along the boundary Γ
an alternative optimisation method is sought. Pursuing the size study a sizing optimisation could
be developed, which optimises orifice size and location. More advanced methods such a level­
set topology optimisation or moving morphable components is also suggested. As these methods
have not been used in this thesis, it is necessary to develop and implement them for pump orifice
optimisation.
52
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:63==
5
Conclusion
It was shown, that the implemented numerical method and optimisation routine yielded optimised
thrust bearing designs similar to those found in literature. This was seen for both infinitely wide
and finite width bearing domains.
It was shown, that the optimisation of a viscous pump, based on hydrodynamic thrust bearings,
was possible. The topography optimisation showed substantial improvement of the volume flow
rate in a viscous pump. However the non mass conserving Reynolds equation requires further
examination to determine the physical feasibility.
The topology optimisation of the viscous pump showed limited success. This was due to exploita­
tion of the boundary conditions and assumptions in the governing equation.
Due to these effects the optimised viscous pump geometries are academic in nature.
5.1
Future work
As the topography optimisation of the bearing domains yielded sufficient results, while topology
optimisation of the plates containing the fluid proved unsuccessful, it is suggested to examine
this problem further. A series of different topology optimisation methods are suggested. The
simplest being a combination of filtering and projection or more advanced methods such as level
set topology optimisation and possibly moving morphable components approach. The challenge
is to overcome the boundary orifice, which the mentioned methods are intended to due by limiting
the formation of small orifices along the boundary.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings

on the derivations covered by Hamrock et al. in [16].

C
General Optimisation
This section gives a brief introduction to the concept of minimisation of a function Φ(γ) subject
to inequality constraints and box constraints, which is representative of the implemented objective
functionals examined in this thesis.
Local and global optima
Figure C.1 shows a general one dimensional non­linear function Φ(γ) on the interval [γa, γb]
which exhibits various stationary points of interest. A stationary point is defined as a point where
the derivative of the function is zero and the different classifications of stationary points are marked
in figure C.1.
Global minimum
Global maximum
Local minima
 b
γ
Φ
γ
 a
γ
Local maxima
Saddle
Figure C.1: A general non­linear function with various stationary points.
A local minima occurs at a gradient changing sign from negative to positive. A local maxima occurs
at a gradient changing sign from positive to negative. A saddle point is defined as a stationary point
with same sign gradients on both sides. In figure C.1 the global maximum is also a local maxima
due to the changing sign of the gradient, but the global minimum is located on the end of the
interval without gradient changing sign from negative to positive, thus the global minimum is not
a local minima.
Convexity
The definition of a convex function is:
βΦ(γ1) + (1 −β)Φ(γ2) ≥Φ(βγ1 + (1 −β)γ2)
(C.1)
Which holds for 0 < β < 1 and any two points γ1 and γ2 in the domain of the function Φ. The
graph of a convex function lies below the straight line between γ1 and γ2 as shown in figure C.2a
while for a non­convex function the graph intersects the straight line as shown in figure C.2b.
Letting > replace ≥in (C.1) yields a strictly convex function.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
63
==PAGE:74==
γ
Φ
Φ(γ1)
bΦ(γ1)+(1-b)Φ(γ2)
Φ(γ2)
γ1
γ2
(a) Simple strictly convex problem.
γ
Φ
Φ(γ1)
Φ(γ2)
γ1
γ2
(b) Non­linear non­convex problem.
Figure C.2: Comparison of convex and non­convex problems.
An important property of convex functions is the existence of a single stationary point which is
the global minimum whereas non­convex functions posses several stationary points not limited to
local minima. This property ensures that the optimisation routine locates the global minimum due
to it being the sole stationary point where ∇Φ = 0. From this is is seen that optimisation of non­
convex functions with respect to the global minimum becomes challenging thus optimisation to
local minima is normally performed, Christensen and Klarbring [11]. Another type of interesting
functions are concave functions Φ which are defined as functions where −Φ is strictly convex.
Looking at the non­convex, non­linear function in figure C.1 it is seen that depending on initial
value of γ optimisation can yield the local minima instead of the global minimum. If the initial
value γinit is to the left of the local maxima the global minimum is found. If γinit is between the
local maxima and saddle point the routine converges to either the local minima or saddle point,
depending on the ability of the optimisation routine to pass the saddle.
Constrained optimisation problem
A general optimisation problem with inequality and box constraints is defined as:
min
γ
:
Φ(γ, s)
(C.2)
s.t. :
gi(γ) ≤0
for i = 1, ..., min
(C.3)
γl
