j
for j = 1, ..., nd
(C.4)
Where γ is the vector of design variables, Φ is the objective function, gi is the meq number of
inequality constraints, γl
j and γu
j are the lower and upper bounds for the box constraints on the nd
number of design variables.
According to Christensen and Klarbring in [11] a local minimum, also global minimum, subject to
constraints is found by the introduction of the Lagranian function:

constraint equations these are also referred to as First­Order Necessary Conditions and are the
basis for many optimisation algorithms, see [22] by Nocedal and Wright.

E
Infinitely wide bearing
This appendix covers the optimised results for the infinitely wide bearing domain. Where possible
the optimised results are compared to analytical solutions by Lord Rayleigh. Figure E.1 shows the
layout and boundary conditions of the infinitely wide bearing problem. The domain consists of a
fluid film between the stationary upper surface a and the lower surface b sliding at velocity ub. The
pressure at the inlet and outlet regions pi and po are set to the ambient pressure, which is chosen
as p = 0. The entire bearing geometry is assumed submersed in fluid, which leads to the inlet and
outlet regions consisting of full lubrication fluid.
z
x

h0
ub
pi

Figure E.1: Schematic illustration of sizes and boundary conditions for the infinitely wide bearing
problem. Light grey denotes the design domain and hd denotes the design height.
The most common bearing design is a fixed­incline bearing, which consists of two flat plates with
a small angle between them as shown with a dashed line in figure E.2. Lord Rayleigh, showed that
a parallel step geometry generated a higher load capacity. Figure E.2 shows a parallel step bearing
as a dotted line. The optimal configuration of parallel step bearing was determined as (E.1) by Lord
Rayleigh. The optimal parallel step geometry as specified by (E.1) is referred to as a Rayleigh step
bearing.

the outlet on surface a.
E.1
Discretisation study
The effects of varying discretisation is investigated with respect to analytical solution to a fixed­
incline slider bearing and a parallel­step slider bearing. Figure E.2 shows both slider bearing ge­
ometries for the investigation. The analytical dimensionless pressure distribution P and volume

(E.2)
Where X = x/l is the dimensionless x coordinate and H0 = h0/hd is the film thickness ratio.
Due to the geometry of the parallel­step slider bearing the pressure gradients are constant in the
inlet and outlet regions, see Hamrock et al. [16]. This leads to a linear variation of pressure with
ambient pressure in the inlet and outlet and a dimensionless peak pressure at the step defined as:
Pm =

(E.4)
The geometry parameters in figure E.1 are set to l = h0 = hd = 1 with ns = 1/2 and fluid
properties are set to ρ = η = 1 for the discretisation study.
Figure E.3 shows the relative errors of the maximum pressure discrepancy p and the volume flow
rates Qi and Qo at the inlet and outlet as well as the run time t for element size in a regular grid.
Here the pressure p is the element field quantity and the volume flow rate at the inlet Qi and outlet
Qo are derivatives of the field quantity thus p is expected to show a higher rate of convergence
compared to Qi and Qo. The element size in figure E.3 is defined as:
E = l

72
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:83==
(a) Fixed­Incline slider bearing
(b) Parallel­Step slider bearing
Figure E.3: Relative errors and run time for varying discretisation of infinitely wide bearing prob­
lem. Where p denotes the relative error of pressure, Qi and Qo denotes the relative errors of volume
flow rate and t denotes the run time.
The convergence rates for the fixed­incline slider bearing shown in figure E.3a agrees with the
expectations for a element size within the interval [10−2; 10−4]. For larger elements the conver­
gence rates are not stabilised as indicated by curvature of graphed data in figure E.3a. As element
size approaches 10−5 the definition of relative error suffers from numerical cancellation error thus
increasing the relative error despite minimal change in the absolute error.
The convergence rates for the parallel­step slider bearing in figure E.3b shows identical conver­
gence rate for both field quantity and derivatives. Due to the constant pressure gradients in the
inlet and outlet regions the pressure distribution consists of linear variations which are exactly de­
picted using linear interpolation thus the effect of discretisation is limited to the discontinues film
thickness at nsl being represented by a C0 continuous height function.
For both slider bearing geometries the computational run time increases for increasing number of
elements N and is unaffected by the fluid film thickness distribution.
The inlet Qi and outlet Qo volume flow rates for both fixed­incline and parallel­step slider bear­
ings are of particular interest due to vastly different convergence behavior for the two bearing
geometries.
The relative error of Qi and Qo are different for the fixed­incline slider bearing which shows that
the conservation of mass for the fixed­incline slider bearing is violated. Whereas the relative error
of Qi and Qo are identical for the parallel­step slider bearing. It is well known that the finite
element method does not conserve physical quantities, Brunetière and Tournerie [8], from figure
E.4 it is seen that the ratio Qo/Qi for the fixed­incline slider bearing converges toward Qo/Qi = 1
for increasing number of elements while the ratio Qo/Qi for the parallel­step slider bearing is
Qo/Qi = 1 for all number of elements examined.

Figure E.5a shows the optimised bearing geometry with respect to maximal load capacity for the
two discretisations employed as well as the analytical optimal geometry. It is observed that the
obtained geometries closely resemble the analytical solution which is expected. A discretisation
dependency is observed in the internal corner of the step as shown in the enlarged section of figure

Further the film thickness just in front of the step shows minimal impact on the load thus the
sensitivities are small.

The pressure distributions for the optimised bearing geometries are shown in figure E.5b. It is

distribution which is expected due the similarity of the obtained geometries. The enlarged section

pressure peak occurs closer to the optimal step location as expected for the Galerkin finite element
method. Figure E.6 shows the development of the load capacity during the optimisation routine.
It is observed that the routine reaches a minima within the first 10 iterations and the remaining
iterations show minimal effect on the load capacity.
In table E.3 the loads of the initial and optimised bearing geometries are compared to the ana­
lytically determined optimal load. As expected the initial bearing shows the largest discrepancy
due to the vastly different geometry. The optimised bearing geometry shows a small discrepancy
with respect to the analytically optimal which is caused by the minute sensitivities as changes in
the fluid film height just in front of the step have minimal impact on the load capacity, see the
geometry in figure E.5a. The effect of varying Rex by varying sliding velocity ub on the opti­
mised geometry is shown in figure E.7. As the Rayleigh step bearing is independent of Rex it is
expected to see no variation in optimised geometry for various Rex. From figure E.7 it is seen that
the optimised geometry is independent of sliding velocity ub which is expected. It is proposed that
varying H0 = h0/sh is the only parameter where the optimised geometry shows dependence on
the numerical value. The effect is expected to be visible for H0 < 1.155 as this limits the domain
to a lower total film thickness h0 + sh than the optimal.

for the unconstrained minimisation. A similar geometry is reached for the constrained minimisa­
tion where the incline is shallower and begins the transition from inlet plateau to outlet plateau
nearly at the same bearing coordinate thus leaving a smaller outlet plateau.

leads to a reduced Couette term in the drag functional, Φdrag, while having a minimal reduction
to the load functional, Φload. Further the inclined step reduces the pressure gradient, ∇p, which
leads to a reduction of the Poiseuille term in the drag functional. Maintaining the minimal film
thickness h0 and reducing the length of the outlet plateau ensures sufficient pressure is generated
while maximising the average film thickness which reduces the drag generated by the Couette term
in the drag functional, Φdrag.
From figure E.8a it is observed that the optimised geometry obtained for the constrained optimisa­
tion problem has small fluctuations in the film thickness in the outlet plateau which is not observed
for the unconstrained optimisation despite the usage of a density filter. It is stipulated that these
fluctuations exists to extend the pressure peak’s length with pressure gradient ∇p = 0 to maximise
pressure p with minimal effect to drag. It is suggested to limit the optimisation to the drag func­
tional Φdrag while enforcing a minimum load via a inequality constraint in a attempt to reduce film
thickness fluctuations at the pressure peak.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
79
==PAGE:90==
F
Finite width bearing
The finite width bearing problem described in this section is compared to analytical solutions for
the infinitely wide bearing and finite width bearing from Hamrock [16], as well as numerical work
by Rohde and McAllister in [23], Buscaglia et al. in [9], Andreasen in [5] and Kalliorinne and
Almqvist in [18].
Figure F.1 shows the layout and boundary conditions of the finite width bearing problem. The
domain consists of a fluid film between the stationary upper surface a and the lower surface b
sliding at velocity ub. The pressure at the inlet and outlet regions pi and po are set to the ambient
pressure the outlet region for the pressure boundary condition is assumed to include both sides and
the rear of the domain. The entire bearing geometry is assumed submersed in fluid which leads
to the inlet and outlet regions consisting of full lubrication fluid. The modified Reynolds number
along the x direction is defined by the bearing length l, sliding velocity ub, the minimum fluid
film height h0, and the fluid density ρ and viscosity η. The modified Reynolds number along the y
direction is defined by the bearing width b, sliding velocity vb, the minimum fluid film height h0,
and the fluid density ρ and viscosity η.
The design domain for the optimisation routine is limited to the stationary surface a with an al­
lowable range of film height as h ∈[h0; hmax] with hmax = hd + h0. As the fluid film height
is implemented with linear elements the height field is C0 continuous which also holds for the
optimised height distribution.
z
x
l
ub
pi
hd
h0
po
y
b
Figure F.1: Schematic illustration of sizes and boundary conditions for the finite width bearing
problem. Light grey denotes the design domain and hd denotes the design height.
F.1
Analytical comparison
The analytical solutions for a finite width slider bearing is limited to describing pressure distribu­
tion between flat parallel plates, Hamrock [16]. Due to this limitation the analytical solutions are
only derived for the parallel step slider bearing geometry shown in figure 3.2. This section aims
at comparing the numerical and analytical pressure distribution in a parallel step slider bearing of
finite width.
In this section the infinitely wide bearing geometry is referred to as 1D and the finite width bearing
geometry is referred to as 2D due to the number of dimensions involved in the respective domains.

compared the analytical solution.

In the Galerkin finite element method it is common to use Gauss integration points for field quan­
tities in elements thus this method is examined. However Lengiewicz et al. showed in [20] that
an alternative distribution of numerical integration points referred to as Lobatto yielded smoother
pressure distributions for the same bearing geometry and mesh thus this alternative method is also
examined.
F.1.1
Discretisation study
The effects of varying discretisation is investigated with respect to analytical solution of the finite
width slider bearing. According to Hamrock et al. in [16] a limited number of analytical solu­
tions exist for finite width slider bearing thus the numerical solutions are essential to study various
geometries of finite width slider bearings.
The effect of discretisation is studied for a parallel step slider bearing with the pressure distribution
as described in section F.1. As the method based on Fourier sine series is limited to the pressure
distribution the relative errors are limited to the pressure distribution for the finite width slider
bearings.
To examine the effect of discretisation on the derivatives of the field quantity the volume flow rate
across the boundary of the domain is calculated numerically. As the analytical solution is limited
to the pressure distribution the examination of the volume flow rate relies on the total change of
volume in the domain is defined as:
Q · n = 0
(F.5)
Figure F.3 it is seen that the relative error of the pressure distribution behaves similar to the relative
error of the infinitely wide slider bearing however the discretisation is not refined until numerical
cancellation due limitations in available physical memory.
(a) Gauss integration points
(b) Lobatto integration points
Figure F.3: Relative errors and run time for varying discretisation of finite width bearing problem.
Where p denotes the relative error of pressure, Q denotes the volume flow across bearing boundary
and t denotes the run time.
From figure F.3 it is observed that the volume flow rate, Q, converges for decreasing element side
length, E, similar to the relative errors for the inlet and outlet volume flow rates for the infinitely
wide slider bearing. For regular square elements with side length E the element area becomes E2.
Similar to the infinitely wide slider bearing the CPU run time, t, is inversely linked to the element
side length, E. Due to the increased number of DOF for the two dimensional finite width slider
bearing finite element model the CPU run time is several order of magnitudes larger.
82
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:93==
The effect of discretisation on the optimised bearing geometry is shown in figure F.4 where the
relative improvement |W −WR|/WR, with optimised load capacity W and load capacity for a
Rayleigh step bearing of finite width WR, appears converging for decreasing element size. As the
element side length E becomes smaller than the density filtering radius R a slight reduction of the
relative improvement is seen. It is expected to seen this reduction as the filter radius R is kept as a
physical constant, instead of element side length related constant. The effect on optimised bearing
geometry is examined in section F.2.
Figure F.4: Relative improvement of load capacity in percent for H0 = 1 and λ = 1. FT indicates
usage of density filter with radius R = b/50 indicated by the dashed line. At E = 1/130 m the
relative improvement deviates which is caused by mesh dependencies.
Comparing the relative improvement obtained with Gauss and Lobatto integration points it is seen
that both methods yields similar values and convergence behaviour, but Gauss converges towards
78.71% while Lobatto is slightly lower at 78.57% it is stipulated that this occurs as Gauss generates
more jagged edges and spikes in the pressure distribution, see Lengiewicz [20]. However the
effect appears so minuscule that Gaussian integration is considered the main numerical integration
method for this thesis.
From figure F.4 it is seen that below a element size of E = b/130, where b is the width of the
bearing domain as shown in figure F.1, the relative improvement appears diverging in a chaotic
manner which indicates mesh dependencies. The mesh independencies are expected to be vanes
in the transition from shroud to step as shown in section F.2.

As shown in figure F.6 the optimised bearing geometry for various combinations of length­to­width

in Rohde and McAllister [23]. From figure F.5 it is seen that the relative improvement tends to a
constant value for length­to­width ratio λ = 1 and λ = 0.5 as H0 →0 whereas λ = 2 has not
converged. Prior to converging the relative improvement for all examined values of λ appears to
have a constant gradient with respect to H0 which indicates that for increasing design height hd the
shoulder height sh utilises the full design height until the dimensionless film thickness H0 = h0/hd
exceeds the optimal ratio h0/sh for a given length­to­width ratio λ. The optimal ratio h0/sh for a
given λ can be approximated as the highest H0 with the converged relative improvement in figure
F.5 where the Rayleigh step ratio is indicated by the dotted vertical line at H0 = 1.155.
From figure F.5 the optimal dimensionless film thickness is estimated as H0 ≈0.9 for λ = 0.5
and H0 ≈0.75 for λ = 1. Since the relative improvement for λ = 2 is not converged with the
examined interval it is estimated as H0 ≈0.3. It appears that the optimal H0 approaches H0 =
1.155 for decreasing length­to­width ratio λ which is expected as the infinitely wide bearing for
which Lord Rayleigh determined the optimal bearing geometry as H0 = 1.155 with step location
ns = 0.7182 can be described as having λ = 0.
It is noted that despite the relatively large filter radius of R = b/50, where b is the bearing width as
shown in figure F.1, the relative improvement for length­to­width ratio λ = 1 is slightly reduced

==PAGE:96==
but the reduction is close to negligible.
Figure F.6 shows the optimised geometries for the three values of λ at three different H0 where the
discretisation was refined to a element size of b/256. It is seen that the optimised geometries can
be qualitatively described as shrouded step bearings which is in agreement with findings of Rohde
and McAllister in [23], Buscaglia et al. in [9], Andreasen in [5] and Kalliorinne and Almqvist in
[18].
Figure F.6: Optimised finite width bearing geometries wrt. Φload. Color represents the design
variable γ where the physical film thickness is h = h0 +γhd. Columns represents length­to­width
ratio λ and rows represents dimensionless film thickness H0.
From figure F.6 it is seen that for low values of λ the geometry approaches that of the Rayleigh step
from the infinitely wide bearing which is expected as λ = 0 is a infinitely wide bearing geometry.
Further low values of λ leads to a larger section of the bearing width b being unaffected by the side
leakages occurring at the edges which is observed as a decreasing width of the shrouds. For higher
values of λ the shape of the inlet plateau approaches a rounded off triangle due to the increasing
area where side leakage occurs which leads to a larger shroud.
It is observed for decreasing dimensionless film thickness H0 the full design height hd is not utilised
for λ = 0.5 and λ = 1 which indicates a optimal H0 = h0/sh is achieved thus a optimal shoulder
height sh exists for the finite width bearing. This observation is in agreement with convergence
of relative improvement shown in figure F.5. It is noted that for λ = 2 and H0 = 0.5 the full
design height hd is employed in agreement with the relative improvement for λ = 2 as well as
the observations by Chow et al. in [10]. Similar to the examined bearing geometry in [10] as
the optimised geometry for λ = 2 and H0 = 0.5 consists of a inclined inlet region with shrouds
terminated by a discrete step into the outlet plateau. It is estimated that the inclined inlet region is
86
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:97==
the primary cause of the higher H0 required for convergence.
For decreasing λ and H0 a series of vanes are seen in the corners between shroud and step in
figure F.6 which was also observed by Andreasen in [5] and Kalliorinne and Almqvist in [18].
The shape and location of the vanes indicates that these are introduced as a mean of side leakages
prevention by prohibiting the y­direction flow at the step which extends the high pressure zone in
the y­direction yielding a higher load capacity. The pressure distributions in figure F.7 shows the
effect of the vanes as inverted corners of the pressure contours indicating the widened high pressure
region at the step. As the vanes become less prominent for increasing H0 and λ the inverted corners
of the pressure contours disappear and the width of the high pressure zone decreases as shoen in
figure F.7.
Figure F.7: Normalised pressure distribution for optimised finite width bearing geometries. Color
represents normalised pressure p/ max(p). Columns represents length­to­width ratio λ and rows
represents dimensionless film thickness H0.
From the pressure distributions in figure F.7 it is seen that for lower values of length­to­width ratio
λ the high pressure zone at the step fills a larger fraction of the bearing width b than for higher
values of λ which shows the effect of increasing side leakage for increasing λ. Due to the shroud
geometry the pressure contours in figure F.7 shows sharp corners at the transition from the inlet
plateau to the shroud while the countors are rounded in the outlet region. Similar behaviour of the
pressure distribution was shown by Rohde and McAllister [23].

Mesh dependency
In figure F.5 the bearing domain with length­to­width ratio λ = 1 with and without density filtering
of design variable, which showed near negligible reduction of the optimised load capacity despite a
relatively large filter radius of R = b/50, where b is the finite width of the bearing domain as shown
in figure F.1. In figure F.8 the finite width bearing with λ = 1 and H0 = 0.45 is optimised for
maximum Φload with filtered and unfiltered design variables from which the changes of topography
is examined for a discretisation with element size b/128.
(a) Unfiltered design variable γ.
(b) Filtered design variable ˜γ.
Figure F.8: Comparison of optimised bearing geometry for maximising Φload with filtered and
unfiltered design variables. Filter radius R = b/50 for filtered design variable ˜γ. Color represents
the design variable γ where the physical film thickness is h = h0 + γhd.
In figure F.8a the unfiltered geometry shows two small vanes similar to the vanes in figure F.6
however for the larger element size of E = b/128 the vanes are less prominent which proves
the mesh dependency shown by Kalliorinne and Almqvist [18]. The inlet region shows a slight
convexity as the center is slightly reduced film height then compared to the height next to the
shroud. The convexity reduces as the plateau approaches the step.
Figure F.8b shows the filtered design which lacks the vanes at the transition from shroud to step.
A smoother transition from inlet region to shroud and outlet region is observed, which is to be
expected due to the nature of weighting functions in the density filter. The convexity of the inlet
plateau observed in figure F.8a is also observed for the filtered design however the shrouds are
wider at the inlet which is expected due to the implementation of the weighting function used by
the density filter.
A interesting and unexpected change of topography introduced by the density filtering is seen in
figure F.8b as ripples in the film height running parallel to the step from the inlet plateau to both
shroud and outlet plateau. The stipulated cause of these ripples are the discrete step seen in the
unfiltered optimal bearing geometry which the density filter was not developed for.

==PAGE:100==
Figure F.10 shows the optimised bearing geometry for various λ and low values of H0 from which
it is seen that the optimised bearing geometry changes the qualitative topography depending on the
value of H0.
Figure F.10: Optimised finite width bearing geometries wrt. Φdrag. Color represents the design
variable γ where the physical film thickness is h = h0 +γhd. Columns represents length­to­width
ratio λ and rows represents dimensionless film thickness H0.
For H0 = 0.6 and H0 = 1.05 in figure F.10 a optimised bearing geometry consisting primarily
of a shrouded inlet plateau with height h0 + hd with a incline at the rear edge with a low length­
to­width ratio. The rear incline acts as the pressure generating mechanism while narrow shrouds
minimises side leakages which aids in improving the pressure generation, as described in section
F.2. The rear corners are elevated to maximum allowable height which is explained by examining
the definition of the drag objective functional restated here as:
Φdrag = −h
2∇p −ηu
h
(F.16)
Examining the pressure distributions for H0 = 0.6 and H0 = 1.05 in figure F.11 it is observed that
the pressure distribution in the rear corners is near zero as is the pressure gradient ∇p. From this
and (F.16) it can be seen that the Poiseuille related term of the drag functional is close to zero as it
is contains the pressure gradient ∇p while the Couette related term contains the local film height
h in the denominator which leads to maximising h minimises drag in the rear corners.
For H0 = 0.15 and λ = 0.5 and λ = 1 a completely different topography is observed in figure
F.10 the prominent inlet plateau is split into a lower inlet plateau and a intermediate plateau located
in front of the incline. Further it is seen that the shrouds are angled inwards near the inlet of the
90
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:101==
optimised bearing geometry. The intermediate plateau is caused by the pressure gradient ∇p →0
just in front of the incline as seen from the pressure contours in figure F.11 thus the Couette related
term in (F.16) is dominating the height distribution leading to maximum allowable height in this
region.
Figure F.11: Normalised pressure distribution for optimised finite width bearing geometries. Color
represents normalised pressure p/ max(p). Columns represents length­to­width ratio λ and rows
represents dimensionless film thickness H0.
From the pressure distributions in figure F.11 it is seen that for increasing H0 the pressure gener­
ating incline affects a smaller part of the bearing domain. This is especially noticeable for higher
values of λ. The large flat inlet plateau seen in figure F.10 combined with the pressure distributions
from figure F.11 indicates that the load capacity for the initial fixed­incline geometry is maintained
by effectively decreasing length­to­width ratio λ of the pressure generating incline while increas­
ing the incline angle which indicates that low values of λ combined with a shrouded incline is the
optimal geometry for reducing drag. Similar findings were described by Lord Rayleigh [21] for
infinitely wide bearings and experimentally determined for finite width bearings by Kettleborough
[19].
The effect of the elevated rear corners are angled shrouds for all examined values of λ and H0 as
the corners are elevated the shrouds are angled inwards to remain functioning as shrouds at the
pressure generating incline. The effect of this on the pressure distributions are seen as triangles in
the rearward corners where the discrete increase of film height leads to a sudden drop in pressure
which can lead to negative pressure or cavitation. As the implementation of Reynolds equation in
this thesis is incapable of handling cavitation a negative pressure is expected.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
91
==PAGE:102==
The pressure distribution of the rearward corner triangles is studied further in section F.4 alongside
the intermediate plateau as both of these phenomena are also observed for minimising coefficient
of friction.
Figure F.12: Comparison of unfiltered design variables γ and filtered design variables ˜γ for λ = 1
and low values of H0. Color represents the design variable where the physical film thickness is
h = h0 + γhd.
The effect of density filtering on the intermediate plateau for low values of H0 and the elevated
rearward corners for all examined values of H0 are examined in figure F.12. For H0 the effect is
limited to a smoothing of the discrete height change from inlet plateau to shroud as expected due
to the weighting function employed in the density filter. The same smoothing is observed for the
discrete transition from shroud to elevated rearward corners. Due to the element size of b/128 the
unfiltered design shows a stepped increase of the shroud width, which smoothed but still visible in
the filtered design.
The effect of density filtering for the optimised bearing geometry for H0 = 0.15 is significant for
the height distribution as seen in figure F.12 but minimal for the relative improvement in figure F.9.
The unfiltered geometry shows a intermediate plateau which occurs due to the dominant term of
(F.16) being related to velocity u near the pressure peak, which is not present for the filtered design.
The shroud of the unfiltered design shows a unusual topography then compared to topographies
obtained for other values of H0 and λ. The transition from inlet plateau height to shroud goes
from inlet plateau γ ∼0.5 to 1 at the shroud step down to zero, which is the shroud height.
The shrouds are angled inwards in the inlet region for the unfiltered design, which is expected to
increase side leakages, however the shrouds of the filtered design are not angled inward and share
the topography of other dessgns thus it is deemed necessary to include filtering then H0 →0 to
ensure mesh independency as well as examining if the assumption that terms of order hc/lc can be
neglected in Navier­Stokes equations. The elevated rearward corners are seen to reduce drastically
in size and the transition from shroud to corner is smoothed as the design variables are filtered for
H0 = 0.15 whereas for H0 = 0.45 the transition is smoothed due to the weighting function of
density filter, but the elevated triangle maintains its unfiltered size and shape.
92
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:103==
The thin strip of maximum allowable height at the transition from inlet plateau to shroud is re­
duced in height by filtering as seen in figure F.12 where the filtered design for H0 = 0.15 shows
slight ripples along the shrouds which is expected to arise due to the nearly discrete height changes
observed for the unfiltered design. The inlet plateau for H0 = 0.15 does not utilise the maxi­
mum allowable height which could indicate a converging optimised bearing geometry as shown
for maximising load in figure F.5 and F.6 however the relative improvement of drag in figure F.9
shows a diverging value.

From the converged values of relative improvement in table F.4 and curves in figure F.13 a clear
relationship between achievable reduction of the coefficient of friction and length­to­width ratio λ
is observed.
Figure F.14 shows the optimised bearing geometry for three values of λ and H0 with a element
size of b/128. It is observed from figure F.6 and F.14 that the optimised geometries with respect
to minimising the coefficient of friction µ are similar to the optimised geometries with respect
to maximising load capacity. The largest difference is the step obtained for Φload are not present
in the optimised bearing geometries instead a incline is observed which is in agreement with the
optimised infinitely wide bearing geometries in figure E.8a.

Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:105==
The shrouds seen in figure F.14 are qualitatively identical to the shrouds obtained then maximising
Φload which is expected as the discrete step from inlet plateau to the shrouds minimises side leak­
ages with minimal impact on drag as the discrete step is oriented nearly parallel to the fluid flow
direction. The prominent vanes observed for low values of λ and H0 in figure F.6 are not present
in the optimised bearing geometries with respect to minimising µ however a combination of low
λ and high H0 shows signs of vanes in figure F.14.
Examining the relative reduction of drag in figure F.9 it is seen that for H0 = 1.5 the reduction is
about 5­10% thus maximising load capacity is the primary method for obtaining a lower coefficient
of friction µ. From figure F.5 it is seen that a improvement of 20­65% is achieved then maximising
load capacity, which reinforces the appearance of vanes in figure F.14.
Another observation from figure F.14 is the rear corners of the outlet plateau are raised to the
maximum allowable film thickness for all examined geometries. When comparing the objective
functionals Φload and Φdrag, restated in (F.20), it is seen that in the rear corners the pressure p and
pressure gradient ∇p are negligible which leads to ηu/h being the dominant term thus maximising
film thickness h minimises the dominant term yielding lower drag without affecting load capacity.
Figure F.15 shows the pressure distributions where it is seen that the pressure in the rear corners are
close to p = 0 which is expected. The optimised geometry is similar to that obtained by Kalliorinne
and Almqvist [18].

From figure F.15 it is seen that the pressure contours of the optimised geometries are smoother
than those for the maximised load capacity shown in figure F.7, which is expected as the optimised
geometry contains a inclined step instead of a discrete step. It is observed that the pressure gradient
is reduced in the inlet region and increased in the outlet region as also observed for the infinitely
wide bearing geometry. Similar to the infinitely wide bearing the pressure gradients are affected
as reducing the adverese pressure gradient and increasing the favorable pressure gradient reduces
drag.
The elevated rear corners shown in figure F.14 shows discrete steps in film thickness from h0 to
h0 + hd which can lead to negative pressures, or cavitation if implemented in the model. The
pressure distribution near the rear corner is examined in figure F.16.
Figure F.16a shows the optimised geometry for λ = 1 and H0 = 0.45 while figure F.16b shows the
corresponding pressure distribution. The location of maximum and minimum normalised pressure
are marked by crosses with the labels pmax and pmin. It is noted that the minimum pressure occur
symmetrically in both rear corners but is only marked in one corner. It is observed that negative
pressure does occur in the rear corners at the discrete step up in film thickness as expected. How­
ever the normalised pressure shows the negative pressure’s numerical value is close to 1 % of the
maximum pressure which is considered negligible.
For increasing λ and H0 it is observed that vanes appear in the elevated rearward corners. From
figure F.5 it is seen that for increasing λ at higher H0 the optimise bearing is unable to match the
Rayleigh step, thus it is stipulated that the vanes appearing in the elevated rearward corners are
present to increase the load capacity.
Another interesting aspect of the optimised bearing geometry is visible in figure F.16a. Just in front
of the inclined step a intermediate plateau with maximum allowable film thickness is observed
similar to the intermediate plateau seen in section F.3. As described for the drag minimisation
problem the intermediate plateau occurs as the Couette related term of (F.16) is dominant as the
pressure gradient ∇p changes sign at the pressure peak. This is further reinforced as the effect of

In figure F.12 it was seen that the introduction of a density filter changed the topography for low
values of H0 to qualitatively similar to the optimised topography for larger values of H0. In figure
F.17 the introduction of a density filter has limited effect on the optimised topography for a bearing
domain with λ = 1 and H0 = 0.15 where the coefficient of friction is minimised. The gradient
∇p’s contribution along the x­direction, ∂p/∂x, is examined in figure F.17 as it is expected to be
∂p/∂x ≈0 in the intermediate plateau. A region with near zero ∂p/∂x is observed with nearly
identical shape, size and location in the bearing domain as the intermediate plateau is seen in figure
F.17 for both unfiltered design γ and filtered design ˜γ, which confirms the theory behind Couette
related term in the drag functional dominating the film height distribution at ∇p ≈0.
As minimising coefficient of friction allows for increasing load capacity the pressure gradients
are larger which leads to a distinct difference between ∇p ≈0 in the intermediate plateau and
∇p > 0 in the inlet plateau thus the intermediate plateau exists for both unfiltered and filtered
design variables. Due to the lower load capacity of the bearing geometries obtained for minimising
drag the difference between gradients in the inlet and intermediate plateaus are smaller which leads
marginal reduction of µ, which vanishes then the transitions are smoothed by the density filter.

==PAGE:108==
G
Viscous pump optimisation
The introduction of a pump orifice in the finite width bearing domain is examined in this section.
Three approaches for optimising the viscous pump based on hydrodynamic thrust bearings are
examined.
The first method is the introduction of various orifices in the finite width bearing subject to a
previously used optimisation problem ­ Maximising load capacity.
The second method is a volume flow rate optimisation problem for the finite width bearing domain
with various orifices.
Lastly a topology optimisation of the plates containing the thin film is conducted.
Figure G.1 shows the layout and boundary conditions of the finite width bearing problem. The
domain consists of a fluid film between the stationary upper surface a and the lower surface b
sliding at velocity ub. The pressure at the inlet and outlet regions pi and po are set to the ambient
pressure. The outlet region for the pressure boundary condition is assumed to include both sides
and the rear of the domain. The entire bearing geometry is assumed submersed in fluid which leads
to the inlet and outlet regions consisting of full lubrication fluid.
The design domain for the optimisation routine is limited to the stationary surface a with an al­
lowable range of film height as h ∈[h0; hmax] with hmax = hd + h0. As the fluid film height
is implemented with linear elements the height field is C0 continuous which also holds for the
optimised height distribution.

problem. Light grey denotes the design domain and hd denotes the design height.

G.1
Simple pump optimisation
In this section the optimised geometry for a finite width bearing with respect to maximising load
capacity for various pump orifice locations and dimensionless film thickness H0 is examined.
The introduction of a pump outlet as a orifice in the finite width bearing is expected to have a
negative impact on the optimised bearing geometry with respect to load capacity. The orifice is
implemented as a region of enforced pressure po = 0 which is described in detail in section 2.1.1.
To further simulate a orifice the film height within the orifice is fixed at maximum allowable film
height h0 + hd and affected nodes are excluded from the optimisation problem. The optimisation
problem for maximising load capacity is restated here:

is a fixed­incline bearing with dimensions, boundary conditions and fluid properties as shown in
table F.1 and F.2. The dimensionless film thickness H0 is listed in table G.2. The length­to­width
ratio λ = 1 is chosen as it was shown to achieve the highest relative improvement with respect to
maximising load capacity in section F.2, thus a similar behavior is expected for the introduction of
orifices.

allows more fluid to enter the bearing domain, which appears to improve the fraction Qo/Qi for
all examined orifice locations. Especially the side location S shows a increase in fraction Qo/Qi
for decreasing H0. However the finite width Rayleigh step bearing with orifices still provide the
highest fraction Qo/Qi for all examined orifice locations.

==PAGE:112==
As the introduction of orifices with enforced pressure po = 0 drastically reduces the pressure in
the Rayleigh step bearing the large relative improvements observed in figure G.5a is still expected
to show lower load capacities than obtained in section 3.2.1 for the full domain without orifices.
For the three orifice locations examined the relative improvement with respect to maximising load
capacity behaves similar to the relative improvement for λ = 1 in figure F.5 which is the same
bearing domain without orifices. However from figure G.5a it is seen that the relative improvement
has a maximum as reducing H0 further leads to a decrease in relative improvement. For decreas­
ing dimensionless film thickness H0 the load capacity is increasing until a optimal H0 = h0/sh is
reached. For the full domain with λ = 1 from section 3.2.1 the optimal dimensionless film thick­
ness was estimated as H0 ≈0.75. For a centered orifice, marker M, the optimal dimensionless
film thickness is estimated as H0 ≈0.6 similar for a rearward orifice, marker R, the estimated
optimal is H0 ≈0.6 and lastly for a asymmetric located orifice, marker S, the estimated optimal
is H0 ≈0.75.
In figure G.5a it is seen that the maximum relative improvement for the centered and rearward
located orifices are 336.3 % and 357.6 % whereas the asymmetric located orifice only reaches
125.1 %. The discrepancy for the asymmetric located orifice is expected as the maximum pressure
for a full domain Rayleigh bearing is located at the intersection of the center line and the step,
which is closer to the locations of the other orifice locations. Examining figure G.5b it is seen
that the asymmetric located orifice has the smallest discrepancy across all examined values of H0,
which then combined with the lower relative improvement indicates that the location has a lower
effect on the load capacity. This is expected due to the location away from the maximum pressure
in the bearing domain.
From figure G.5b it is observed that the asymmetric located orifice and rearwards located orifice
shows relatively constant discrepancy then compared to the optimised load capacities for the full
domain. The centered orifice, marker M, shows a larger discrepancy at higher values of H0, which
is rapidly decreasing for decreasing values of H0 until a discrepancy of ∼19 % is reached for
H0 ≤0.6. This discrepancy is of similar size as the remaining orifice locations examined.
Figure G.6 shows the optimised bearing geometry for maximising load capacity with pump outlet
orifices in various location and for specific values of dimensionless film thickness H0 ∈{0.5, 1, 2}.
These values of H0 are chosen as these depicts the optimised topography at various points of inter­
est. At H0 = 0.5 the curves in figure G.5a appears converged, thus the bearing design with optimal
h0/sh is examined. The non­converged optimised bearing designs are examined at H0 = 1 and
H0 = 2. At H0 = 2 the highest discrepancy for the orifice marked by M occurs, as shown in
figure G.5b. The number of elements is increased leading to a element size of E = b/300 in figure
G.6.
From figure G.6 it is observed that qualitatively the optimised bearing designs attempts to surround
the orifices with a region of film height h0, which is an effort to minimise the impact of the enforced
pressure of po = 0 on pressure distributions, shown in figure G.7. Generally the optimised bearing
designs are best described as modified shrouded step bearings, which is expected as it is the same
optimisation problem used to generate these bearing geometries as for the full domains described
in section 3.2.1. Vanes similar to those observed in section 3.2.1 are present in the majority of the
optimised bearing geometries seen in figure G.6.
Examining the optimised geometry for the centered circular orifice, marker M, in figure G.6 it is
seen that a shrouded parallel step bearing geometry is present with a island of height h0 around the
orifice for all examined values of dimensionless film thickness H0. For H0 = 0.45 it is observed
that the inlet plateau is not utilising the full design height hd indicating that a optimal H0 = h0/sh
exists. This is in agreement with the findings of figure G.5a.
102
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:113==
Figure G.6: Optimised finite width bearing geometries wrt. Φload. Color represents the design
variable γ where the physical film thickness is h = h0 + γhd. Columns represents orifice location
and rows represents dimensionless film thickness H0. Blue circle marks edge of orifice.
As observed in section 3.2.1 vanes in the transition from shroud to step is observed for decreasing
H0 however the introduction of a orifice with po = 0 changes the vanes to be small and more
numerous.
It is expected that the orifice marked by M reduces the pressure at the step. This is shown to
affect the step location in figure G.6. The large vanes observed in section F.2 in the shroud to step
transition are not present in figure G.6. It is expect that the vanes are less efficient due to a reduce
pressure gradient along y­direction in the bearing domain. From the pressure distributions in figure
G.7 it is seen that the high pressure zone at the step is slightly narrower than in figure F.7. This
further supports the idea, that vanes are less efficient due to the reduced pressure gradient along
y­direction.
For decreasing values of H0 the island surrounding the orifice appears converging towards an island
at film height h0 with a surrounding at film height h0 + hd. This leads to a pressure increase, thus
reducing the effect of the enforced pressure po = 0 on the surrounding pressure distribution. In
figure G.7 it is seen, that pressure contours are minimally affected outside the island. For H0 = 2
the rear section of the island surrounding the orifice shows two large vanes, in the opposite direction
of vanes seen in other optimised designs, with an increasing incline directly behind the orifice.
A similar optimised design was shown with a square orifice by Buscaglia et al. in [9]. From
the pressure distribution in figure G.7 it appears that the vanes separate the higher pressure on
both sides of the island from pushing fluid towards the region behind the orifice as this leads to
a pressure distribution with a lower variation across the bearing domain at the cost of a reduced
average pressure leading to a lower load capacity.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
103
==PAGE:114==
Figure G.7: Normalised pressure distribution for optimised finite width bearing geometries. Color
represents normalised pressure p/ max(p). Columns represents orifice location and rows repre­
sents dimensionless film thickness H0. Blue circle marks edge of orifice.
Examining the optimised design and pressure distribution for the rearward located orifice, marker
R, it is seen that the full design height for H0 = 0.5 is not utilised which indicates that a optimal
dimensionless thickness H0 = h0/sh exists. As the rearward location of the orifice is near the
location of the discrete step between the inlet and outlet plateaus it affects the shape of the step in
the x­y plane as seen in figure G.6 for all examined values of H0. Qualitatively the step geometry
can be described as a forward bulge with a bell shaped region of elevated film height behind the
orifice.
For increasing values of H0 the bulge section of the step takes up a larger fraction of the total
width of the step and for H0 = 2 a series of small vanes are observed. These vanes are oriented
perpendicular to the pressure contours from figure G.7. This leads to the vanes being angled at
∼30°to the direction of movement for surface b.
The bell shape behind the orifice is used for letting fluid flow in behind the orifice to allow pressure
generation at the step from bell to outlet plateau. This is seen as a small strip of higher pressure at
the rear edge of the bell in figure G.7. For decreasing values of H0 the bell shape narrows as the
step between inlet and outlet plateau moves backwards thus narrowing the bulge, but the effect of
the bell remains.
Similar to the centered orifice a small region of maximum allowable film height is observed in
front of the bulge for H0 = 0.5 which serves the same purpose of increasing pressure near the
orifice thus effectively reducing the low pressure zone from the enforced pressure po = 0. The
same effect is seen along the rear edge of the bell which is also maximising the pressure generation
at the discrete step from bell to outlet plateau.
104
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:115==
From figure G.6 it is seen that the optimised bearing designs with a asymmetric located orifice,
marker S, shows significantly different topography for the examined values of H0.
For larger values of the dimensionless film thickness H0 a topography similar to that observed
for the rearward located orifice exists. The topography is asymmetric but shows a shrouded inlet
plateau with a discrete step to a outlet plateau with a bulge in front of the orifice as well as s
asymmetric bell behind the orifice.
At H0 = 2 a vane is seen within the bell. The right side of the bell goes forward in the bearing
domain and contacts the boundary Γ. This appears to be a smaller shrouded step bearing form from
the edge of domain Ωand the rear edge of the bell.
The pressure distribution in figure G.7 shows that within the bell a typical pressure distribution of
a small shrouded step bearing exists. On a larger scale it is observed that the close proximity of the
orifice and outer edge leads to a large zone of nearly zero pressure between the orifice and edge
thus only three quarters of the bearing width b is able to use the total domain length l to generate
a large pressure peak at the step.
For H0 = 1 the topography is largely unchanged but vanes are observed at the transition from
shroud to step and the vane in front of the orifice is larger. The shape of the bell is different but
still resembles one half of a shrouded step bearing. The most significant change to topography is
the bulge connecting the outer edge at boundary Γ with the vane in front of the orifice is thinner
and a small vane in the opposite direction is seen at the orifice.
The reverse vane separates the low pressure zone at the orifice from the higher pressure generated
by the bell as seen in figure G.7. Similarly the larger vane in front of the bulge enables a higher
pressure in the larger region of the inlet plateau by effectively splitting the pressure distribution
in two as can be seen by the sharp corners on pressure contours. The thinner bulge allows for a
longer bell plateau which in turn leads to a higher pressure at the transition between bell and outlet
plateau.
Lastly for H0 = 0.5 the bulge is not connected to the shroud instead one large vane split the
bearing domain from λ = 1 in to two smaller domains with higher effective length­to­width ratios.
The larger effective bearing is not utilising the full design height hd which indicates a optimal
dimensionless film thickness H0 = h0/sh. The geometry remains a shrouded parallel step with
vanes in the transition from shroud to step.
The smaller effective bearing is utilising the full design height which is expected as for large values
of λ a mixed incline and step bearing is optimal as shown by Chow et al. in [10].

G.2
Volume flow rate
Introducing a circular orifice in the center of a bearing domain similar to marker M in figure G.3 and
table G.1 with a orifice radius Ro = b/10 and considering the optimisation problem in (G.5)­(G.7)
it is possible to compare the two methods for calculating the volume flow rate per element.
max
γ
:
ΦQ(γ, p)
(G.5)
s.t. :
R(γ, p) = 0
(G.6)
0 ≤γi ≤1
for i = 1, ..., nd
(G.7)
where ΦQ(γ, p), R(γ, p), γi and nd are defined in section 2.3 A domain with bearing width b =
1 m and length­to­width ratio λ = 1 with h0 = hd = 1 m is used for examining the optimised
bearing designs with the full nodal consistent numerical integration and the optimised bearing
design obtained with the under integration.
Figure G.8 compares the optimised bearing designs obtained with the full nodal consistent numer­
ical integration and with under integration. Full nodal consistent integration and under integration
are described in appendix D.4.
The optimised geometry for full nodal consistent integration shows a chevron shaped island with
film height h0 located behind the orifice. This indicates the fluid being redirected towards the ori­
fice as shown by the pressure distribution with overlaid streamlines in figure G.8. Further a thin
shroud along the boundary Γ is observed in figure G.8, which upon closer examination appears to
be located on the second row of nodes along the front half of bearing domain Ω.
The thin shroud generates a region of negative pressure which generates a favorable pressure gra­
dient at the inlet thus sucking in more fluid. A identical shrouds occurs for the front half of the
sides in the bearing domain, which serves the same purpose of generating suction at boundary Γ.
The suction along the sides appears visually as the streamlines angled inwards along both sides.
The thin shrouds along the sides are replaced by two smaller islands that generates negative pres­
sure via a discrete increase of film height near the outlet which reduces the volume flow rate across
the outlet at the cost small regions of positive pressure in front of the islands.
The small regions of positive pressure appears to generate a small side leakage however the nega­
tive pressure near the outlet reduces the volume flow rate out of the bearing domain more than the
increased side leakage increases volume flow rate.
Due to assumptions in the Reynolds equation negative pressure is a viable solution, but physically
it is not a viable solution. As the pressure reduces below the fluid’s vapor pressure it is expected
to observe cavitation. This is not accounted for in the standard reduced form of the Reynolds
equation. Lengiewicz et al. [20] extended the Reynolds equation to account for cavitation, which
proved successful. The proposed cavitation model is a iterative solver, that is far more complicated
and time consuming, than the linear Reynolds equation. To avoid this complexity the method of
under integration is examined.
106
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:117==
Figure G.8: Bearing design variable γ and pressure p with streamlines for a discretisation with
element side length E = b/64. For γ color represents the design variable where the physical film
thickness is h = h0 + γhd. The red line represents the edge of the orifice and blue lines represent
streamlines.
The under integration is shown as the right column in figure G.8 and shows the optimised bearing
design and pressure distribution for the same optimisation problem. The optimised bearing design
shows a vastly different topography where the primary contribution is the central orifice. This can
be seen by the large inlet plateau and the pressure distribution with streamlines showing a large
area of fluid being redirected towards the orifice instead of generating suction at the boundary Γ.
At the forward corners two small vanes exists, those purpose is to redirect flow at the inlet towards
the center line. This reduces side leakage and increases the fluid flowing towards the central orifice.
The shape of the transition from inlet plateau to outlet plateau is similar to a shrouded step bearing
which is expected as the shrouded bearing minimises side leakage while maximising the pressure
at the intersection of the step and center line where the orifice is located.
The film height is increased at the sides for the first row of nodes and two small regions of maximum
allowable film height is seen in the outlet plateau. This acts as suction generation to reverse the
flow direction at the sides in the outlet region.
For both designs a small island is seen in the center of the inlet which is a numerical artifact
occurring at the symmetry line as symmetry has been used to reduce computational demands.
Similar a small region of maximum height is seen in the outlet region for the design obtained via
under integration of volume flow rate, which also related to the usage of symmetry.
The optimisation problem is solved for three levels of discretisation and both numerical integration
methods. The obtained designs are qualitatively identical to those shown in figure G.8. Table G.3

Table G.3: Comparison of dimensionless volume flow rates for full integration and under integra­
tion of volume flow rates.
From table G.3 it is seen that the volume flow rates for the initial geometry is fluctuating as expected
as the pressure distribution is not converged due to the coarse discretisation as shown in appendix
F.1.1. A similar fluctuation is observed for the volume flow rate of the optimised bearing designs.
However the relative improvement of the volume flow rates is significantly higher for the nodal
consistent numerical integration then compared to the under integration of the volume flow rate.
Combining the bearing designs and pressure distributions from figure G.8 with the relative im­
provements of table G.3 it is observed that the nodal consistent implementation exploits assump­
tions and weakness of Reynolds equation and the numerical implementation. This leads to far
greater volume flow rates than those obtained by the under integration. Examining the definition
of the volume flow rate per unit width stated as:
q = −h3
12∇p + hub
2
(G.8)
it is seen that for a favorable pressure gradient, ∇p < 0, both terms on the right hand side increases
with increasing film height h. Combining volume flow rate per unit width with the locations of
integration points in figure D.3, the results obtained in figure G.8 is related to the assumptions in
Reynolds equation and the numerical implementation.
For nodal consistent numerical integration the only nodal values evaluated in the calculation of vol­
ume flow rate are located on boundary Γ, thus maximising film height h and minimising pressure
gradient ∇p on boundary Γ leads to the maximising volume flow rate Q into the bearing domain.
To obtain this for the inlet region of bearing domain Ωthe film height of nodes on boundary Γ
utilises full design height. The second row of nodes minimises film height and the inner region
of the bearing domain Ωutilises full film height which creates a negative pressure, which in turn
increases −∇p on boundary Γ.
For under integration of volume flow rate all nodal values of film height h in elements on boundary
Γ affects the volume flow rate, which leads to a different optimised bearing design as seen in figure
G.8. The primary change in topography is the lack of thin shrouds along the boundary Γ as this
negatively affects the volume flow rate in the inner nodes of elements on the boundary.
Instead the orifice is used to increase the volume flow rate into the boundary which leads to a pres­
sure distribution that is primarily positive instead of negative.
A negative pressure causes cavitation, which would lead to a pressure of zero. Therefor the op­
timised geometry with consistent nodal integration is not physically feasible whereas the under
integration remains physically feasible. Another observation is the fewer iterations of optimisa­
tion required for the under integration, which indicates a optimisation problem that is closer to
being convex.
108
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings

orifices described in table G.4. The optimisation problem for maximising load capacity is restated

Solving the optimisation problem for the five orifices with varying H0 ∈{0.15, 2.1} leads to
the curves in figure G.10a. From figure G.10a it is seen that the qualitative behavior of Qo/Qi
is similar for all examined orifices and appears diverging for decreasing values of dimensionless
film thickness H0.
For values of H0 > 0.45 the asymmetric located orifice S shows the highest fraction Qo/Qi
followed by the remaining circular orifices. The square orifices are ∼30% lower than the circular
orifices for H0 > 0.45. For the narrow square a distinct kink is seen in the curve at H0 = 1.2,
which indicates a drastic change in topography of the optimised design.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
109
==PAGE:120==
(a) Qo/Qi for optimised bearing designs.
(b) Relative improvement of Qo/Qi with respect
to Rayleigh step with orifice.
Figure G.10: Qo/Qi and relative improvement of Qo/Qi for optimised bearing designs with ori­
fices as shown in figure 3.20 and H0 ∈{0.15, 2.1}.
Examining the pressure distributions in figure G.12 it is seen that the circular orifices marked by M
and S have minimal pressure in front of the orifices which leads to a higher volume flow rate into
the inlet plateau. Further the higher pressure surrounding the orifices guides more fluid towards
the orifices. For the other orifices the pressure in front of the orifices reduces the volume flow rate
in to the orifices. This is particularly easy to see for the wide square marked by Sq.W in figure
G.12, where a high pressure zone is present in front of the orifice. The high pressure pushes fluid
towards the edges instead of letting it flow in to the orifice.
For lower values of H0 the volume flow rate fraction Qo/Qi appears to rapidly approach unity in
figure G.10a. The extremely high volume flow rate fractions for low values of H0 are expected to
be the result of three effects:
The volume flow rate Qi is limited to the volume flow rate in through the inlet side, whereas the
actual volume flow rate into the bearing domain also includes potential suction occurring on the
sides.
The physical feasibility of the Reynolds equation for large shoulder heights sh is questionable due
to the possibility of swirls developing in front of the step.
Lastly the Reynolds equation is not mass conserving, which is especially relevant for coarser dis­
cretisation.
Comparing the optimised designs in figure G.11 it is suggested to implement a finer discretisation
as the bearing designs shows limited signs of suction along the sides. Further the designs do not
appear to redirect all fluid towards the orifices, which indicates that the lack of mass conservation
is the cause of large volume flow rate fractions.
The relative improvement of volume flow fraction Qo/Qi in figure G.10b shows, that the cen­
tered orifice M achieves a significantly higher improvement than the other examined orifices. For
all examined optimised pump designs a diverging relative improvement for low values of H0 is
observed.
Figure G.11 shows a selection of optimised bearing designs with the five orifices. The optimised
bearing designs can be qualitatively described as shrouded step bearings with inclusions in the inlet
plateau. The optimised bearing designs are obtained for a bearing domain as shown in figure G.2
with geometric and fluid properties from table F.1 and boundary conditions from table F.2. The
orifices are introduced as described in table G.1 and table G.4.

==PAGE:121==
Figure G.11: Optimised finite width bearing geometries wrt. ΦQ. Color represents the design
variable γ where the physical film thickness is h = h0 + γhd. Columns represents orifice location
and rows represents dimensionless film thickness H0 = [0.45, 1.05, 2.1]. Blue line marks edge
of orifice.
From figure G.11 it is seen that the orifice edge is exposed to the full design height for the orifice
marked S. For the orifice marked M the front ∼40% orifice edge is exposed. The remaining orifices
are either surrounded by minimum film height h0 or the front half is minimum film height h0. The
open orifices of marker M and S are of particular interest and are examined further in section 4.3.2.
The narrow square orifice marked by Sq.N and the wide square orifice marked by Sq.W have same
area as the circular orifices and are described in table G.4 as well as shown in figure G.9.
The narrow square orifice shows a similar bearing design as observed in appendix G.1 for the
rearward circular orifice, where the bell shaped region is described.
The pressure distributions in figure G.12 shows that the pressure in front of the orifice is lowest for
the asymmetric located orifice marked by S. The highest pressure in front of the orifice is observed
for the centrally located circular orifice M and wide square Sq.W. It is expected that the lower
pressure in front of the orifice allows more fluid to enter, which is shown in figure G.10a.

111
==PAGE:122==
Figure G.12: Normalised pressure distribution for optimised finite width bearing geometries.
Color represents normalised pressure p/ max(p). Columns represents orifice location and rows
represents dimensionless film thickness H0 = [0.45, 1.05, 2.1]. Blue line marks edge of orifice.
From the optimised bearing designs in figure G.11 and pressure distributions in figure G.12 it is
seen, that the topography of all orifice locations changes for low values of H0. The narrow square
orifice shows significantly different topography for low values of H0. For H0 = 0.45 it is seen
that the topography exposes ∼30% of the orifice to the inlet region. For higher values of H0 the
orifice is surrounded by a bulge of film height h0. This change in topography is in agreement with
the kinked curve in figure G.10a.
Unexpectedly the topography for the optimised design with the wide square orifice is dependent
on the value of H0. For low values of H0 the orifice is surrounded by low film height while the
bearing resembles a shrouded step bearing. For intermediate values of H0 an island in front of the
orifice is observed. Lastly for large values of H0 the bearing domain is essentially split into two
domain at the orifice. This leads to two consecutive bearing domains with a length­to­width ratio
of λ ≈0.5.
For the centered circular orifice, marked by M, the optimised bearing design shows a small island
around the orifice. For decreasing H0 the front edge of the orifice becomes increasingly exposed to
the inlet region. Maximising load capacity led to a optimal shoulder height sh, which is observed
behind the orifice. In front of the orifice the full design height hd is utilised to ensure maximum
volume flow rate in to the bearing domain Qi. Similar optimised bearing designs are observed for
the orifices marked by R and S.

G.4
Pump topology optimisation
In this section the optimised results for maximising the volume flow rate into a bearing domain
Ωfor a finite width bearing geometry is examined. A fixed orifice is replaced by the Brinkman
inspired penalty term for various values of maximum penalty number αmax. The optimisation
problem is defined as:
max
γ
:
ΦQ(γ, p)
(G.13)
s.t. :

(G.14)

load

A∗−1 ≤0

where ΦQ(γ, p), Φload(γ, p), R(γ, p), γi and nd are defined in section 2.3. Φ∗
load is a minimum
allowable load capacity. A is the surface area of surface a acting as orifice in bearing domain Ω
and A∗is the maximum allowed surface area acting as a orifice.
The initial bearing geometry is a fixed­incline bearing with dimensions, boundary conditions and
fluid properties as shown in table F.1 and F.2. The dimensionless film thickness H0 = 1 and
the length­to­width ratio λ = 1 is chosen. The initial topology of plate a is a solid plate. This
enforced with ξn = 0 for all nodes. The minimum load capacity is specified as W = Winit/2
where Winit is the load capacity for the initial bearing geometry. To examine the behavior of the
topology optimisation of plate a a exceptionally large orifice area A∗= A/10 is allowed.
Letting αmin = 0 and αmax ∈{10−6, 102} with a penalty factor of k = 1000 the volume flow
rates for the optimised bearing geometries are shown in figure G.13, where the results are obtained
without continuation approach. The optimised topology and pressure distributions are shown in
figure G.14 to G.17.
Figure G.13: Volume flow rate into boundary Γ for optimised bearing plate topology and number
of iteration required for αmax ∈{10−6, 102}.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
113
==PAGE:124==
From figure G.13 it is seen that for increasing values of αmax the volume flow rate Q converges to
a constant value of 615.23 mm3 s−1.
For low values of αmax the optimised orifice is located at the pressure peak and its shape is defined
by the pressure contours. This can be seen for αmax = 10−6 where the orifice is shown in figure
G.14 and corresponding pressure distribution in figure G.15. For αmax ≥100 the orifice is located
along the inlet and sides. This is related to the inlet velocity profile across the fluid film height.
The inlet velocity profile across the fluid film height consists of a constant Couette term and a
adverse Poiseuille term. The Poiseuille term occurs due to a adverse pressure gradient, which
exists due to the pressure generation of the fixed incline topography. Due to the adverse pressure
gradient the Poiseuille term reduces the volume flow rate in to the inlet, thus it is beneficial to
reduce the numerical value of the term. This explains the orifice location for high values of αmax,
since the high penalty number enforces the pressure po = 0.
Figure G.14: Optimised finite width bearing plate a. Color represents the design variable ξ where
the penalty value is α = αmin + ξαmax.
Considering αmax < 100 the penalty number is insufficient, thus a non zero pressure is present
above the orifice as shown in figure G.15. Locating a orifice in a high pressure zone leads to a
higher volume flow rate Qo. Low values of αmax leads to a weakly coupled problem, where the
pressure distribution affects the orifice location. In this problem the orifice has minimal impact on
the pressure distribution.
For αmax ∈{10−5, 10−1} a series of interesting optimised bearing plates a are seen in figure
G.14 where the transition from a weak to strong coupling between orifice penalty and pressure
distribution affects the topology. Qualitatively the change in topology can be described as for
increasing αmax the optimal orifice shape and location follows decreasing pressure contours until
114
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:125==
the boundary Γ with enforced pressure of p = 0 is reached. It is seen that the topology in the
orifice rings are intermittent solid and orifice and for αmax = 10−1 a series of single node orifices
are seen near the outlet.
Figure G.15: Dimensionless pressure for optimised plate topology with unfiltered design variables
ξ. Color represents dimensionless pressure.
The fluctuations between 1 and 0 for the unfiltered design variables ξ leads to uneven pressure
distribution which is most noticeable for αmax = 10−5 and αmax = 10−1 whereas the pressure dis­
tributions for αmax = 10−6 and αmax ≥100 are smooth. Despite the uneven pressure distributions
it is observed from figure G.15 that the transitional values of αmax yields the highest maximum
pressure. This is expected due to the coupling being strong enough to move the orifices away from
the pressure peak but weak enough to not enforce a pressure of p = 0 at ξ = 1.
Introducing a density filter on the design variables is expected to have the greatest effect for the
transitional values of αmax as the weak and strong coupling lead to optimised designs without local
fluctuations of design variables. Figure G.16 shows the optimised topology of plate a with filtered
design variables ˜ξ where the density filter is used with radius R = b/50 and b = 50 mm.
From figure G.16 a similar relationship between optimised topology and value of αmax is observed
for the filtered design variables ˜ξ. For lower values of αmax a combination of using symmetry
to reduce the computational domain and filtering introduces a center line of solid, that effects the
topology. For αmax = 10−6 the center line effect is the only change to the optimised topology as
expected due the lack small features in the unfiltered topology.
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
115
==PAGE:126==
Figure G.16: Optimised finite width bearing plate a. Color represents the filtered design variable
˜ξ where the penalty value is α = αmin + ˜ξαmax.
The narrow rings seen in the unfiltered design for transitional values of αmax from figure G.14 are
replaced by a few larger regions of ˜ξ = 1 in the filtered design. For αmax = 10−5 and αmax = 10−4
the orifices are split into a frontal row and a rear row. This leads to a relatively low pressure around
the boundary Γ while maintaining a large region of high pressure contained by the orifices as seen
in figure G.17.
For αmax = 10−3 the pressure contour shaped orifice reappears. The contour is not closed, but
instead of consisting of intermittent orifice a solid, continuous orifice is observed.
For αmax = 10−2 a complete pressure contour shape is seen, that acts in a similar manner as the
topology for αmax = 10−3.
At a value of αmax = 10−1 the qualitative topology remains unaltered by the introduction of
filtering. The topology of several small orifices distributed near the boundary Γ is expected to be
caused by the transition from weak to strong coupling. This is supported by the orifice along the
inlet boundary and sides.
116
Topology optimization of hydrodynamic bearings
==PAGE:127==
Figure G.17: Dimensionless pressure for optimised plate topology with filtered design variables
˜ξ. Color represents dimensionless pressure.
To ensure the orifices are simulated as holes instead of porous media the value of αmax is required
to be large, however this leads to unwanted orifice along inlet and sides of domain Ω. A continu­
ation approach was employed in an attempt to avoid the orifice along inlet and sides of domain Ω.
The continuation approached increased αmax, however it proved futile as the converged bearing
topology was identical to that obtained by a constant αmax.
Other methods used by the author are reduced control volume for determining volume flow rates
and reduced design domain. The obtained result were similar to those covered in this appendix.
Instead of locating the orifice along the inlet to the bearing domain the orifice was located along
either the boundary of the design domain or control volume.

Element matrices for FEM

1 of 1
function [ME,AE,FE]=planeMatrix(coords,h,a,u,eta,numint)
% coords is element coordinates
% p is pressure [2x1]
% eta is absolute viscosity
% ME is element matrix
 
ME=zeros(4); % System matrix
AE=zeros(4); % Penalty matrix
FE=zeros(4,1); % Force vector
 
if strcmp(numint,'G')
    gp=[-1/sqrt(3), 1/sqrt(3)];
    w=[1,1];
elseif strcmp(numint,'L')
    gp=[-1, 1];
    w=[1,1];
elseif strcmp(numint,'U') % Under relaxed Gauss
    gp=0;
    w=2;
else
    disp('Wrong selection of integration')
    gp=[0,0];
    w=[0,0];
end
 
for i=1:size(gp,2)
    for j=1:size(gp,2)
        xi=[gp(i),gp(j)];
        N=[(1-xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), (1+xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), (1+xi(1))*(1+xi(2)), (1-xi(1))*
(1+xi(2))]/4;
        Nu=[(1-xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi(1))*(1+xi(2)), 0, 
(1-xi(1))*(1+xi(2)), 0;
            0, (1-xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi(1))*(1+xi(2)), 0, 
(1-xi(1))*(1+xi(2))]/4;
        dN=[-1+xi(2), 1-xi(2), 1+xi(2), -1-xi(2);
            -1+xi(1), -1-xi(1), 1+xi(1), 1-xi(1)]/4;
        J=dN*coords; % From Cook - 6/4
        detJ=J(1,1)*J(2,2)-J(2,1)*J(1,2); % Samme som det() - 6/4
        invJ=1/detJ*[J(2,2), -J(1,2); -J(2,1), J(1,1)]; % samme som inv() - 6/4
        B=invJ*dN;
        Bu=invJ*[-1+xi(2),0,1-xi(2),0,1+xi(2),0,-1-xi(2),0;
                 0,-1+xi(1),0,-1-xi(1),0,1+xi(1),0,1-xi(1)];
        ME=ME+w(i)*w(j)*detJ*B'*(N*h)^3*B;
        %AE=AE+w(i)*w(j)*detJ*N*a; % NOT working as intended 30/6
        FE=FE-w(i)*w(j)*detJ*6*eta*(N'*(Nu*u)'*B*h+N'*(N*h)*sum(Bu*u));
    end
end
AE=diag(a);
 
end

% FEM Reynolds equation
% Extended to 2D - 29/3-22 ABP
function [sol,f0,df0,f,df]=film_2D(fem,design,designA)
% fem.X is node list
% fem.IX is connectivity matrix
% fem.bc.... is boundary conditions structure
%    .bc_pressure           is nodes with defined pressure
%    .bc_pressure_values    is corresponding pressure values
 
nel=size(fem.IX,1);
nn=length(fem.X);
ndof=nn;
 
edofMat=fem.IX; % Due to 1 dof per node
 
if size(fem.u,2)==2
    uConst=fem.u;
    fem.u=zeros(2*nn,1);
    fem.u(1:2:end)=uConst(1);
    fem.u(2:2:end)=uConst(2);
end
 
[h,dh]=interpolate(fem,design);
if fem.outlet
    [alpha,dalpha]=interpolateAlpha(fem,designA);
else
    alpha=zeros(nn,1);
end
 
[G,N, Null]=boundary_conditions(fem);
 
[M,A,F]=formResidualJacobian(fem,edofMat,h,alpha);
 
F=F-M*G;
M = Null'*M*Null - (Null-speye(ndof,ndof));
F(N~=1)=G(N==0);
% M=M+A; % Test location
sol=M\F;
 
if nargout>1
    if strcmp(fem.int,'G')
        gp=[-1/sqrt(3), 1/sqrt(3)];
        w=[1,1];
    elseif strcmp(fem.int,'L')
        gp=[-1, 1];
        w=[1,1];
    elseif strcmp(fem.int,'U')
        gp=0;
        w=2;
    else
        disp('Wrong selection of integration')
        keyboard
    end
    switch fem.objective
        case 'force' % FD check approved - 14/4

(uedof))'*B*[0 1 0 0]'+6*fem.eta*Np'*(Np)*sum(Bu*fem.u(uedof))*[0 1 0 0]');
                        dload_3(elem)=dload_3(elem)+w(i)*w(j)*detJ*dh(edof(3))*adj
(edof,1)'*(B'*3*(Np*h(edof))^2*B*(Np*[0 0 1 0]')*sol(edof)+6*fem.eta*Np'*(Nu*fem.u
(uedof))'*B*[0 0 1 0]'+6*fem.eta*Np'*(Np)*sum(Bu*fem.u(uedof))*[0 0 1 0]');
                        dload_4(elem)=dload_4(elem)+w(i)*w(j)*detJ*dh(edof(4))*adj
(edof,1)'*(B'*3*(Np*h(edof))^2*B*(Np*[0 0 0 1]')*sol(edof)+6*fem.eta*Np'*(Nu*fem.u
(uedof))'*B*[0 0 0 1]'+6*fem.eta*Np'*(Np)*sum(Bu*fem.u(uedof))*[0 0 0 1]');
                    end
                end
                dload(edofMat(elem,:))=dload(edofMat(elem,:))+[dload_1(elem);dload_2
(elem);dload_3(elem);dload_4(elem)];

            df0=-dload;
            f=1;
            df=zeros(size(df0));
        case 'friction' % FD check approved - Gauss and force
            L=zeros(nn,3);
            dragConst=[0 0];
            for elem=1:nel
                edof=edofMat(elem,:);
                coords=fem.X(fem.IX(elem,:),:);
                uedof=[edof(1)*2-1,edof(1)*2,edof(2)*2-1,edof(2)*2,edof(3)*2-1,edof(3)
*2,edof(4)*2-1,edof(4)*2]; % Variable velocity field 24/5
                for i=1:length(gp)
                    for j=1:length(gp)
                        xi=[gp(i),gp(j)];
                        Np=[(1-xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), (1+xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), (1+xi(1))*(1+xi
(2)), (1-xi(1))*(1+xi(2))]/4;
                        Nu=[(1-xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi(1))*
(1+xi(2)), 0, (1-xi(1))*(1+xi(2)), 0;
                            0, (1-xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi(1))*(1-xi(2)), 0, (1+xi
(1))*(1+xi(2)), 0, (1-xi(1))*(1+xi(2))]/4;
                        dN=[-1+xi(2), 1-xi(2), 1+xi(2), -1-xi(2);
                            -1+xi(1), -1-xi(1), 1+xi(1), 1-xi(1)]/4;
                        J=dN*coords; % From Cook - 6/4
                        detJ=J(1,1)*J(2,2)-J(2,1)*J(1,2); % Samme som det() - 6/4
                        invJ=1/detJ*[J(2,2), -J(1,2); -J(2,1), J(1,1)]; % samme som 
inv() - 6/4
                        B=invJ*dN;
                        %Bu=invJ*[-1+xi(2),0,1-xi(2),0,1+xi(2),0,-1-xi(2),0;
                        %    0,-1+xi(1),0,-1-xi(1),0,1+xi(1),0,1-xi(1)];
                        L(edof,1)=L(edof,1)+w(i)*w(j)*detJ*Np';
                        L(edof,2:3)=L(edof,2:3)+w(i)*w(j)*detJ*(-.5*Np*h(edof)*B)';
                        dragConst=dragConst+w(i)*w(j)*detJ*(Nu*fem.u(uedof))'*fem.eta/
(Np*h(edof));
                    end
                end
            end
            
            load=L(:,1)'*sol;
            drag_x=L(:,2)'*sol-dragConst(1);

1]'/detJ);

(edof,1)'* (B'*3*(Np*h(edof))^2*Np*[1 0]'*B*sol(edof)/detJ^2+const*Np'*B*[1 0]'/detJ);
